\documentclass[11pt]{article}

    \usepackage[breakable]{tcolorbox}
    \usepackage{parskip} % Stop auto-indenting (to mimic markdown behaviour)
    

    % Basic figure setup, for now with no caption control since it's done
    % automatically by Pandoc (which extracts ![](path) syntax from Markdown).
    \usepackage{graphicx}
    % Keep aspect ratio if custom image width or height is specified
    \setkeys{Gin}{keepaspectratio}
    % Maintain compatibility with old templates. Remove in nbconvert 6.0
    \let\Oldincludegraphics\includegraphics
    % Ensure that by default, figures have no caption (until we provide a
    % proper Figure object with a Caption API and a way to capture that
    % in the conversion process - todo).
    \usepackage{caption}
    \DeclareCaptionFormat{nocaption}{}
    \captionsetup{format=nocaption,aboveskip=0pt,belowskip=0pt}

    \usepackage{float}
    \floatplacement{figure}{H} % forces figures to be placed at the correct location
    \usepackage{xcolor} % Allow colors to be defined
    \usepackage{enumerate} % Needed for markdown enumerations to work
    \usepackage{geometry} % Used to adjust the document margins
    \usepackage{amsmath} % Equations
    \usepackage{amssymb} % Equations
    \usepackage{textcomp} % defines textquotesingle
    % Hack from http://tex.stackexchange.com/a/47451/13684:
    \AtBeginDocument{%
        \def\PYZsq{\textquotesingle}% Upright quotes in Pygmentized code
    }
    \usepackage{upquote} % Upright quotes for verbatim code
    \usepackage{eurosym} % defines \euro

    \usepackage{iftex}
    \ifPDFTeX
        \usepackage[T1]{fontenc}
        \IfFileExists{alphabeta.sty}{
              \usepackage{alphabeta}
          }{
              \usepackage[mathletters]{ucs}
              \usepackage[utf8x]{inputenc}
          }
    \else
        \usepackage{fontspec}
        \usepackage{unicode-math}
    \fi

    \usepackage{fancyvrb} % verbatim replacement that allows latex
    \usepackage{grffile} % extends the file name processing of package graphics
                         % to support a larger range
    \makeatletter % fix for old versions of grffile with XeLaTeX
    \@ifpackagelater{grffile}{2019/11/01}
    {
      % Do nothing on new versions
    }
    {
      \def\Gread@@xetex#1{%
        \IfFileExists{"\Gin@base".bb}%
        {\Gread@eps{\Gin@base.bb}}%
        {\Gread@@xetex@aux#1}%
      }
    }
    \makeatother
    \usepackage[Export]{adjustbox} % Used to constrain images to a maximum size
    \adjustboxset{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}

    % The hyperref package gives us a pdf with properly built
    % internal navigation ('pdf bookmarks' for the table of contents,
    % internal cross-reference links, web links for URLs, etc.)
    \usepackage{hyperref}
    % The default LaTeX title has an obnoxious amount of whitespace. By default,
    % titling removes some of it. It also provides customization options.
    \usepackage{titling}
    \usepackage{longtable} % longtable support required by pandoc >1.10
    \usepackage{booktabs}  % table support for pandoc > 1.12.2
    \usepackage{array}     % table support for pandoc >= 2.11.3
    \usepackage{calc}      % table minipage width calculation for pandoc >= 2.11.1
    \usepackage[inline]{enumitem} % IRkernel/repr support (it uses the enumerate* environment)
    \usepackage[normalem]{ulem} % ulem is needed to support strikethroughs (\sout)
                                % normalem makes italics be italics, not underlines
    \usepackage{soul}      % strikethrough (\st) support for pandoc >= 3.0.0
    \usepackage{mathrsfs}
    

    
    % Colors for the hyperref package
    \definecolor{urlcolor}{rgb}{0,.145,.698}
    \definecolor{linkcolor}{rgb}{.71,0.21,0.01}
    \definecolor{citecolor}{rgb}{.12,.54,.11}

    % ANSI colors
    \definecolor{ansi-black}{HTML}{3E424D}
    \definecolor{ansi-black-intense}{HTML}{282C36}
    \definecolor{ansi-red}{HTML}{E75C58}
    \definecolor{ansi-red-intense}{HTML}{B22B31}
    \definecolor{ansi-green}{HTML}{00A250}
    \definecolor{ansi-green-intense}{HTML}{007427}
    \definecolor{ansi-yellow}{HTML}{DDB62B}
    \definecolor{ansi-yellow-intense}{HTML}{B27D12}
    \definecolor{ansi-blue}{HTML}{208FFB}
    \definecolor{ansi-blue-intense}{HTML}{0065CA}
    \definecolor{ansi-magenta}{HTML}{D160C4}
    \definecolor{ansi-magenta-intense}{HTML}{A03196}
    \definecolor{ansi-cyan}{HTML}{60C6C8}
    \definecolor{ansi-cyan-intense}{HTML}{258F8F}
    \definecolor{ansi-white}{HTML}{C5C1B4}
    \definecolor{ansi-white-intense}{HTML}{A1A6B2}
    \definecolor{ansi-default-inverse-fg}{HTML}{FFFFFF}
    \definecolor{ansi-default-inverse-bg}{HTML}{000000}

    % common color for the border for error outputs.
    \definecolor{outerrorbackground}{HTML}{FFDFDF}

    % commands and environments needed by pandoc snippets
    % extracted from the output of `pandoc -s`
    \providecommand{\tightlist}{%
      \setlength{\itemsep}{0pt}\setlength{\parskip}{0pt}}
    \DefineVerbatimEnvironment{Highlighting}{Verbatim}{commandchars=\\\{\}}
    % Add ',fontsize=\small' for more characters per line
    \newenvironment{Shaded}{}{}
    \newcommand{\KeywordTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.00,0.44,0.13}{\textbf{{#1}}}}
    \newcommand{\DataTypeTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.56,0.13,0.00}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\DecValTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.25,0.63,0.44}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\BaseNTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.25,0.63,0.44}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\FloatTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.25,0.63,0.44}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\CharTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.25,0.44,0.63}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\StringTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.25,0.44,0.63}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\CommentTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.38,0.63,0.69}{\textit{{#1}}}}
    \newcommand{\OtherTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.00,0.44,0.13}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\AlertTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{1.00,0.00,0.00}{\textbf{{#1}}}}
    \newcommand{\FunctionTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.02,0.16,0.49}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\RegionMarkerTok}[1]{{#1}}
    \newcommand{\ErrorTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{1.00,0.00,0.00}{\textbf{{#1}}}}
    \newcommand{\NormalTok}[1]{{#1}}

    % Additional commands for more recent versions of Pandoc
    \newcommand{\ConstantTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.53,0.00,0.00}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\SpecialCharTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.25,0.44,0.63}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\VerbatimStringTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.25,0.44,0.63}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\SpecialStringTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.73,0.40,0.53}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\ImportTok}[1]{{#1}}
    \newcommand{\DocumentationTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.73,0.13,0.13}{\textit{{#1}}}}
    \newcommand{\AnnotationTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.38,0.63,0.69}{\textbf{\textit{{#1}}}}}
    \newcommand{\CommentVarTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.38,0.63,0.69}{\textbf{\textit{{#1}}}}}
    \newcommand{\VariableTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.10,0.09,0.49}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\ControlFlowTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.00,0.44,0.13}{\textbf{{#1}}}}
    \newcommand{\OperatorTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.40,0.40,0.40}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\BuiltInTok}[1]{{#1}}
    \newcommand{\ExtensionTok}[1]{{#1}}
    \newcommand{\PreprocessorTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.74,0.48,0.00}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\AttributeTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.49,0.56,0.16}{{#1}}}
    \newcommand{\InformationTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.38,0.63,0.69}{\textbf{\textit{{#1}}}}}
    \newcommand{\WarningTok}[1]{\textcolor[rgb]{0.38,0.63,0.69}{\textbf{\textit{{#1}}}}}
    \makeatletter
    \newsavebox\pandoc@box
    \newcommand*\pandocbounded[1]{%
      \sbox\pandoc@box{#1}%
      % scaling factors for width and height
      \Gscale@div\@tempa\textheight{\dimexpr\ht\pandoc@box+\dp\pandoc@box\relax}%
      \Gscale@div\@tempb\linewidth{\wd\pandoc@box}%
      % select the smaller of both
      \ifdim\@tempb\p@<\@tempa\p@
        \let\@tempa\@tempb
      \fi
      % scaling accordingly (\@tempa < 1)
      \ifdim\@tempa\p@<\p@
        \scalebox{\@tempa}{\usebox\pandoc@box}%
      % scaling not needed, use as it is
      \else
        \usebox{\pandoc@box}%
      \fi
    }
    \makeatother

    % Define a nice break command that doesn't care if a line doesn't already
    % exist.
    \def\br{\hspace*{\fill} \\* }
    % Math Jax compatibility definitions
    \def\gt{>}
    \def\lt{<}
    \let\Oldtex\TeX
    \let\Oldlatex\LaTeX
    \renewcommand{\TeX}{\textrm{\Oldtex}}
    \renewcommand{\LaTeX}{\textrm{\Oldlatex}}
    % Document parameters
    % Document title
    \title{Chapter3}
    
    
    
    
    
    
    
% Pygments definitions
\makeatletter
\def\PY@reset{\let\PY@it=\relax \let\PY@bf=\relax%
    \let\PY@ul=\relax \let\PY@tc=\relax%
    \let\PY@bc=\relax \let\PY@ff=\relax}
\def\PY@tok#1{\csname PY@tok@#1\endcsname}
\def\PY@toks#1+{\ifx\relax#1\empty\else%
    \PY@tok{#1}\expandafter\PY@toks\fi}
\def\PY@do#1{\PY@bc{\PY@tc{\PY@ul{%
    \PY@it{\PY@bf{\PY@ff{#1}}}}}}}
\def\PY#1#2{\PY@reset\PY@toks#1+\relax+\PY@do{#2}}

\def\PYZbs{\char`\\}
\def\PYZus{\char`\_}
\def\PYZob{\char`\{}
\def\PYZcb{\char`\}}
\def\PYZca{\char`\^}
\def\PYZam{\char`\&}
\def\PYZlt{\char`\<}
\def\PYZgt{\char`\>}
\def\PYZsh{\char`\#}
\def\PYZpc{\char`\%}
\def\PYZdl{\char`\$}

    % For linebreaks inside Verbatim environment from package fancyvrb.
    \makeatletter
        \newbox\Wrappedcontinuationbox
        \newbox\Wrappedvisiblespacebox
        \newcommand*\Wrappedvisiblespace {\textcolor{red}{\textvisiblespace}}
        \newcommand*\Wrappedcontinuationsymbol {\textcolor{red}{\llap{\tiny$\m@th\hookrightarrow$}}}
        \newcommand*\Wrappedcontinuationindent {3ex }
        \newcommand*\Wrappedafterbreak {\kern\Wrappedcontinuationindent\copy\Wrappedcontinuationbox}
        % Take advantage of the already applied Pygments mark-up to insert
        % potential linebreaks for TeX processing.
        %        {, <, #, %, $, ' and ": go to next line.
        %        _, }, ^, &, >, - and ~: stay at end of broken line.
        % Use of \textquotesingle for straight quote.
        \newcommand*\Wrappedbreaksatspecials {%
            \def\PYGZus{\discretionary{\char`\_}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\char`\_}}%
            \def\PYGZob{\discretionary{}{\Wrappedafterbreak\char`\{}{\char`\{}}%
            \def\PYGZcb{\discretionary{\char`\}}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\char`\}}}%
            \def\PYGZca{\discretionary{\char`\^}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\char`\^}}%
            \def\PYGZam{\discretionary{\char`\&}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\char`\&}}%
            \def\PYGZlt{\discretionary{}{\Wrappedafterbreak\char`\<}{\char`\<}}%
            \def\PYGZgt{\discretionary{\char`\>}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\char`\>}}%
            \def\PYGZsh{\discretionary{}{\Wrappedafterbreak\char`\#}{\char`\#}}%
            \def\PYGZpc{\discretionary{}{\Wrappedafterbreak\char`\%}{\char`\%}}%
            \def\PYGZdl{\discretionary{}{\Wrappedafterbreak\char`\$}{\char`\$}}%
            \def\PYGZhy{\discretionary{\char`\-}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\char`\-}}%
            \def\PYGZsq{\discretionary{}{\Wrappedafterbreak\textquotesingle}{\textquotesingle}}%
            \def\PYGZdq{\discretionary{}{\Wrappedafterbreak\char`\"}{\char`\"}}%
            \def\PYGZti{\discretionary{\char`\~}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\char`\~}}%
        }
        % Some characters . , ; ? ! / are not pygmentized.
        % This macro makes them "active" and they will insert potential linebreaks
        \newcommand*\Wrappedbreaksatpunct {%
            \lccode`\~`\.\lowercase{\def~}{\discretionary{\hbox{\char`\.}}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\hbox{\char`\.}}}%
            \lccode`\~`\,\lowercase{\def~}{\discretionary{\hbox{\char`\,}}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\hbox{\char`\,}}}%
            \lccode`\~`\;\lowercase{\def~}{\discretionary{\hbox{\char`\;}}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\hbox{\char`\;}}}%
            \lccode`\~`\:\lowercase{\def~}{\discretionary{\hbox{\char`\:}}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\hbox{\char`\:}}}%
            \lccode`\~`\?\lowercase{\def~}{\discretionary{\hbox{\char`\?}}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\hbox{\char`\?}}}%
            \lccode`\~`\!\lowercase{\def~}{\discretionary{\hbox{\char`\!}}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\hbox{\char`\!}}}%
            \lccode`\~`\/\lowercase{\def~}{\discretionary{\hbox{\char`\/}}{\Wrappedafterbreak}{\hbox{\char`\/}}}%
            \catcode`\.\active
            \catcode`\,\active
            \catcode`\;\active
            \catcode`\:\active
            \catcode`\?\active
            \catcode`\!\active
            \catcode`\/\active
            \lccode`\~`\~
        }
    \makeatother

    \let\OriginalVerbatim=\Verbatim
    \makeatletter
    \renewcommand{\Verbatim}[1][1]{%
        %\parskip\z@skip
        \sbox\Wrappedcontinuationbox {\Wrappedcontinuationsymbol}%
        \sbox\Wrappedvisiblespacebox {\FV@SetupFont\Wrappedvisiblespace}%
        \def\FancyVerbFormatLine ##1{\hsize\linewidth
            \vtop{\raggedright\hyphenpenalty\z@\exhyphenpenalty\z@
                \doublehyphendemerits\z@\finalhyphendemerits\z@
                \strut ##1\strut}%
        }%
        % If the linebreak is at a space, the latter will be displayed as visible
        % space at end of first line, and a continuation symbol starts next line.
        % Stretch/shrink are however usually zero for typewriter font.
        \def\FV@Space {%
            \nobreak\hskip\z@ plus\fontdimen3\font minus\fontdimen4\font
            \discretionary{\copy\Wrappedvisiblespacebox}{\Wrappedafterbreak}
            {\kern\fontdimen2\font}%
        }%

        % Allow breaks at special characters using \PYG... macros.
        \Wrappedbreaksatspecials
        % Breaks at punctuation characters . , ; ? ! and / need catcode=\active
        \OriginalVerbatim[#1,codes*=\Wrappedbreaksatpunct]%
    }
    \makeatother

    % Exact colors from NB
    \definecolor{incolor}{HTML}{303F9F}
    \definecolor{outcolor}{HTML}{D84315}
    \definecolor{cellborder}{HTML}{CFCFCF}
    \definecolor{cellbackground}{HTML}{F7F7F7}

    % prompt
    \makeatletter
    \newcommand{\boxspacing}{\kern\kvtcb@left@rule\kern\kvtcb@boxsep}
    \makeatother
    \newcommand{\prompt}[4]{
        {\ttfamily\llap{{\color{#2}[#3]:\hspace{3pt}#4}}\vspace{-\baselineskip}}
    }
    

    
    % Prevent overflowing lines due to hard-to-break entities
    \sloppy
    % Setup hyperref package
    \hypersetup{
      breaklinks=true,  % so long urls are correctly broken across lines
      colorlinks=true,
      urlcolor=urlcolor,
      linkcolor=linkcolor,

    \hypertarget{chapter-3-optimization-methods-in-image-processing-and-computer-vision}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Chapter 3: Optimization Methods in
Image Processing and Computer
Vision}}{Chapter 3: Optimization Methods in Image Processing and Computer Vision}}\label{chapter-3-optimization-methods-in-image-processing-and-computer-vision}}

    In the realm of modern image processing and computer vision, many
fundamental tasks can be elegantly formulated as \textbf{optimization
problems} or \textbf{inverse problems}. Whether the goal is to restore a
blurred image, recover missing pixels, enhance resolution, or segment
meaningful structures, the underlying mathematical principle often
involves finding the \emph{most probable} or \emph{most regularized}
solution to an ill-posed or noisy system. This chapter delves into a
collection of such optimization-driven techniques that form the backbone
of advanced image restoration, reconstruction, and analysis.

\hypertarget{the-role-of-optimization-in-image-processing}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{The Role of Optimization in Image
Processing}}{The Role of Optimization in Image Processing}}\label{the-role-of-optimization-in-image-processing}}

Images captured in real-world scenarios are seldom perfect --- they are
corrupted by blur, noise, missing information, or resolution
limitations. Recovering the original scene from these degraded
observations constitutes an \textbf{inverse problem}, typically
\emph{ill-posed} due to incomplete or noisy data. Optimization methods
provide a systematic framework to handle these challenges by
incorporating \textbf{data fidelity terms} (ensuring consistency with
observations) and \textbf{regularization priors} (embedding assumptions
about natural images such as smoothness, sparsity, or structural
continuity).

Optimization forms the mathematical foundation of nearly all modern
image processing and computer vision algorithms. Tasks such as
denoising, deblurring, inpainting, segmentation, registration, and
super-resolution can often be expressed as \emph{energy minimization} or
\emph{inverse problems}. In general, given an observed (possibly
corrupted) image \(f\), we wish to estimate a clean or restored image
\(u\) by minimizing an energy functional of the form
\(E(u) = D(u, f) + \lambda R(u)\), where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(D(u, f)\) is the \textbf{data fidelity term}, ensuring that \(u\)
  remains close to the observed image \(f\).
\item
  \(R(u)\) is the \textbf{regularization term}, imposing prior knowledge
  or smoothness constraints on \(u\).
\item
  \(\lambda > 0\) is a \textbf{regularization parameter} controlling the
  balance between fidelity and smoothness.
\end{itemize}

The simplest problem is \textbf{non-blind deblurring}, where the blur
kernel (or point spread function) is known. Classical linear methods
such as \textbf{Truncated Singular Value Decomposition (TSVD)} and
\textbf{Tikhonov regularization} offer stable solutions by constraining
the ill-conditioned inverse of the blur operator. The
\textbf{Constrained Least Squares (CLS)} approach and its iterated
variants further refine this by balancing fidelity and smoothness in the
spatial domain. When the blur kernel itself is unknown, the task becomes
one of \textbf{blind deconvolution}. Algorithms like the
\textbf{Richardson--Lucy method} tackle this challenge iteratively,
estimating both the image and the blur sibetaltaneously.

\hypertarget{optimization-beyond-deblurring}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Optimization Beyond
Deblurring}}{Optimization Beyond Deblurring}}\label{optimization-beyond-deblurring}}

Optimization plays a pivotal role in many other domains beyond
deblurring.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Image colorization}, for instance, can be formulated as an
  optimization problem over color smoothness and structural coherence,
  where chrominance is propagated from known regions to unknown areas
  based on luminance similarity.
\item
  \textbf{Image reconstruction} from partial or noisy data often
  leverages \textbf{Minimum Mean Square Error (MMSE)} estimation to
  yield statistically optimal results.
\item
  \textbf{Image denoising} and \textbf{inpainting} can be modeled as
  \emph{variational} or \emph{proximal optimization} problems, where
  priors such as the \textbf{Lasso (ℓ₁ sparsity)} or \textbf{Total
  Variation (TV)} promote edge-preserving smoothness.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{solving-inverse-problems-with-modern-optimization-algorithms}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Solving Inverse Problems with
Modern Optimization
Algorithms}}{Solving Inverse Problems with Modern Optimization Algorithms}}\label{solving-inverse-problems-with-modern-optimization-algorithms}}

Contemporary optimization algorithms have dramatically advanced
computational imaging. Methods such as \textbf{ISTA (Iterative
Shrinkage-Thresholding Algorithm)}, \textbf{FISTA (Fast ISTA)}, and
\textbf{ADMM (Alternating Direction Method of multipliers)} are at the
heart of solving large-scale, high-dimensional inverse problems
efficiently. These frameworks are widely used in applications such as
compressed sensing, image denoising, and deblurring, enabling
high-quality restoration with theoretical convergence guarantees.

The same principles extend naturally to \textbf{image inpainting}, where
missing pixels are filled in based on structural and sparsity priors.
Sparse coding and \textbf{Orthogonal Matching Pursuit (OMP)}, for
example, exploit redundant dictionaries to reconstruct textures or edges
from incomplete observations.

\hypertarget{optimization-in-structural-manipulation-and-segmentation}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Optimization in Structural
Manipulation and
Segmentation}}{Optimization in Structural Manipulation and Segmentation}}\label{optimization-in-structural-manipulation-and-segmentation}}

Optimization frameworks are not limited to restoration---they also
enable content-aware image editing and segmentation. \textbf{Seam
Carving} uses energy minimization to intelligently resize images or
remove objects while preserving important structures. \textbf{Image
Quilting}, a patch-based optimization method, synthesizes realistic
textures by minimizing boundary inconsistencies between overlapping
regions.

In the realm of segmentation, \textbf{Graph-Cut optimization} provides a
powerful interactive framework for delineating objects based on pixel
similarity and boundary costs, while \textbf{Efficient Graph-Based
Segmentation} algorithms use spectral and local energy minimization for
fast, unsupervised image partitioning.

This chapter thus weaves together a spectrum of optimization-based
techniques---from classical regularized inversion methods to modern
sparse and graph-based formulations. Each section introduces both the
mathematical foundations and practical implementations of these methods,
supported by Python demonstrations and visual results.

Through this exploration, readers will gain not only a deeper
understanding of optimization as a unifying language in image processing
but also the computational tools to apply these principles across
restoration, enhancement, and analysis tasks.

The chapter concludes by highlighting the unifying role of optimization
across diverse image processing tasks---from deblurring and inpainting
to segmentation and texture synthesis. Readers are encouraged to
recognize optimization not just as a mathematical tool, but as a
conceptual bridge connecting inverse problems, machine learning, and
modern computational imaging.

    \hypertarget{classical-least-squares-and-regularized-inverse-problems}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{1. Classical Least-Squares and
Regularized Inverse
Problems}}{1. Classical Least-Squares and Regularized Inverse Problems}}\label{classical-least-squares-and-regularized-inverse-problems}}

Classical least-squares methods provide a foundational framework for
solving inverse problems in image processing by estimating unknown
images from degraded observations through error minimization. When such
problems are ill-posed or noise-sensitive, regularization techniques
introduce prior constraints that stabilize the solution and yield
meaningful reconstructions.

\hypertarget{non-blind-deblurring}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{1.1 Non-Blind
Deblurring}}{1.1 Non-Blind Deblurring}}\label{non-blind-deblurring}}

Image deblurring (or deconvolution) is a fundamental \textbf{inverse
problem} in image processing. When an image \(f\) is blurred by a known
point spread function (PSF) or blur kernel \(H\), and corrupted by
noise, the observed image (\(g\)) can be modeled as \(g = Hf + n\).
where

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(f\) is the original (unknown) sharp image,
\item
  \(g\) is the observed blurred image,
\item
  \(H\) is the linear blur operator (or convolution matrix),
\item
  \(n\) is additive noise.
\end{itemize}

The goal of \textbf{non-blind deblurring} is to recover \(f\) given both
\(g\) and \(H\). In practice, this inverse problem is
\textbf{ill-posed}, meaning small amounts of noise in \(g\) can lead to
large errors in the solution \(f\). Therefore, \emph{regularization} is
crucial to obtain stable and visually plausible reconstructions.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{truncated-singular-value-decomposition-tsvd-and-tikhonov-regularization-ridge-regression-filter}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{1.1.1 Truncated Singular Value
Decomposition (TSVD) and Tikhonov Regularization (Ridge Regression
Filter)}}{1.1.1 Truncated Singular Value Decomposition (TSVD) and Tikhonov Regularization (Ridge Regression Filter)}}\label{truncated-singular-value-decomposition-tsvd-and-tikhonov-regularization-ridge-regression-filter}}

A natural reconstruction approach is to estimate \(f\) by solving the
least-squares problem \(\underset{f}{\min}|Hf - g|_2^2\). The
corresponding normal equations yield the pseudo-inverse solution
\(f = H^{+} g\). However, this approach is highly unstable in practice,
because the matrix \(H\) is typically \textbf{ill-conditioned}: its
singular values decay rapidly toward zero, causing severe amplification
of noise components associated with small singular values. To stabilize
the inversion, \textbf{regularization} is introduced. Two classical and
closely related strategies are \textbf{Tikhonov regularization} and
\textbf{Truncated Singular Value Decomposition (TSVD)}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{tikhonov-regularization-ridge-regression}{%
\subparagraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Tikhonov Regularization (Ridge
Regression)}}{Tikhonov Regularization (Ridge Regression)}}\label{tikhonov-regularization-ridge-regression}}

Tikhonov regularization augments the least-squares objective with a
penalty on the solution norm:
\(\underset{f}{\min} |Hf - g|_2^2 + \lambda^2 |f|_2^2\), where
\(\lambda > 0\) is the regularization parameter controlling the
trade-off between data fidelity and solution smoothness: \(\lambda\)
controls the smoothness (larger \(\lambda\) → smoother image). The
closed-form solution is given by
\(f = (H^T H + \lambda^2 I)^{-1} H^T g\). From a numerical perspective,
the regularization term improves the conditioning of the inverse problem
by preventing the amplification of components associated with small
singular values. From a statistical viewpoint, this formulation
corresponds to a \textbf{MAP estimator} under a Gaussian prior on \(f\),
favoring solutions with small energy.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{truncated-singular-value-decomposition-tsvd}{%
\subparagraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Truncated Singular Value
Decomposition
(TSVD)}}{Truncated Singular Value Decomposition (TSVD)}}\label{truncated-singular-value-decomposition-tsvd}}

An alternative stabilization strategy is provided by TSVD, which
explicitly exploits the \textbf{singular value decomposition}
\(H = U \Sigma V^T, \quad \Sigma = \mathrm{diag}(\sigma_1, \sigma_2, \ldots, \sigma_n)\),
with singular values ordered as
\(\sigma_1 \ge \sigma_2 \ge \cdots \ge \sigma_n \ge 0\). In the absence
of regularization, the pseudo-inverse solution can be expressed as
\(f = \sum\limits_{i=1}^{n} \frac{u_i^T g}{\sigma_i} v_i\), which
clearly reveals the instability when \(\sigma_i\) is small. TSVD
mitigates this issue by \textbf{discarding} the components corresponding
to singular values below a chosen threshold \(\lambda\):
\(f = \sum\limits_{i:,\sigma_i > \lambda} \frac{u_i^T g}{\sigma_i} v_i\).
This truncation suppresses high-frequency (unstable) components
(corresponding to near-zero singular values) that are poorly supported
by the data and dominated by noise, yielding a more stable
reconstruction.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{interpretation-and-comparison}{%
\subparagraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Interpretation and
Comparison}}{Interpretation and Comparison}}\label{interpretation-and-comparison}}

Both Tikhonov regularization and TSVD address the ill-posedness of
non-blind deblurring by controlling the influence of small singular
values. Tikhonov regularization applies a \textbf{continuous
attenuation} to all components, while TSVD performs a \textbf{hard
cutoff} in the spectral domain. Despite their different formulations,
both methods can be interpreted as spectral filtering techniques and
form the foundation of more advanced regularization and
optimization-based deblurring methods discussed later in this chapter.
Both methods are best understood through the \textbf{Singular Value
Decomposition (SVD)} of the blur matrix \(H = U \Sigma V^T\).

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{matrix-based-2d-deblurring}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Matrix-Based 2D
Deblurring}}{Matrix-Based 2D Deblurring}}\label{matrix-based-2d-deblurring}}

For 2D images, we can represent the blur as separable matrices \(A\) and
\(B\), such that \(G = BFA^T + N\), where \(G\) is the observed blurred
image, \(F\) is the true image, and \(N\) is noise. The deblurring
process is applied by computing SVDs of \(A\) and \(B\), and
reconstructing the image in the transformed (frequency-like) domain. The
next figure illustrates the restoration process:
\includegraphics{images/tikhonov.png}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{python-demonstration-and-explanation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Python Demonstration and
Explanation}}{Python Demonstration and Explanation}}\label{python-demonstration-and-explanation}}

Below is a Python implementation of \textbf{non-blind deblurring} using
\textbf{Tikhonov} and \textbf{TSVD filters}. It illustrates how
matrix-based regularization methods can restore a degraded image when
the blur operator is known. Let's go through it step by step:

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{read-input-image}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Read input
image}}{Read input image}}\label{read-input-image}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Reads the grayscale version (\texttt{0} flag) of the sample image
  (\texttt{book.png}).
\item
  The image matrix \texttt{F} represents the \textbf{original clean
  image} --- our \emph{ground truth}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{construct-the-blur-operators}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Construct the Blur
Operators}}{Construct the Blur Operators}}\label{construct-the-blur-operators}}

Here we construct two \textbf{circulant matrices} \texttt{A} and
\texttt{B} to represent \textbf{1D blurs} along the image columns and
rows respectively.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A \emph{circulant matrix} is one where each row is a cyclic shift of
  the previous one, mimicking a \textbf{linear convolution} operation.
\item
  The \textbf{logarithmic spacing} (\texttt{np.logspace}) defines
  gradually changing blur strengths to make the degradation more
  realistic.
\item
  The combination of forward and reversed arrays ensures symmetric blur.
\end{itemize}

Mathematically, the observed image \(G\) is modeled as:
\(G = BFA^T + N\) where \(N\) is additive noise.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{create-the-degraded-image}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Create the Degraded
Image}}{Create the Degraded Image}}\label{create-the-degraded-image}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The matrix multiplications \texttt{B\ @\ F\ @\ A.T} apply the
  \textbf{row and column blur operators} to the original image
  \texttt{F}.
\item
  The term \texttt{10*np.random.random(...)} adds \textbf{Gaussian-like
  noise} to sibetalate sensor noise.
\item
  The resulting image \texttt{G} is the \textbf{blurred and noisy
  observation}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{visualize-the-setup}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Visualize the
Setup}}{Visualize the Setup}}\label{visualize-the-setup}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Plots the \textbf{original image},
\item
  The \textbf{blur matrices} \texttt{A} and \texttt{B},
\item
  And the resulting \textbf{degraded image} \texttt{G}.
\end{itemize}

This helps visualize how structured blur and noise affect image quality.

\PY{n}{B} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{circulant}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{32} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{logspace}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mf}{1e\PYZhy{}2}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{96}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{tolist}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n+nb}{list}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{reversed}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{logspace}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mf}{1e\PYZhy{}3}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{96}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{tolist}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{+} \PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{32}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Generate degraded (blurred + noisy) image}
\PY{n}{G} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{B} \PY{o}{@} \PY{n}{F} \PY{o}{@} \PY{n}{A}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{T} \PY{o}{+} \PY{l+m+mi}{10}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{random}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{random}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{prod}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{F}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{reshape}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{F}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Visualization of degradation}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{figure}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{figsize}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{20}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{5}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{gray}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{subplots\PYZus{}adjust}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mf}{0.95}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mf}{0.05}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mf}{0.05}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{subplot}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{141}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imshow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{F}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{original}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{size}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{20}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{subplot}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{142}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imshow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{A}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{matrix A}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{size}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{20}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{subplot}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{143}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imshow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{matrix B}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{size}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{20}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{subplot}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{144}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imshow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{G}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{degraded}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{size}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{20}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{show}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_3_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{singular-value-decomposition-svd}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Singular Value Decomposition
(SVD)}}{Singular Value Decomposition (SVD)}}\label{singular-value-decomposition-svd}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{scipy.linalg.svd(A)} decomposes matrix \texttt{A} as
  \(A = U_A \Sigma_A V_A^T\), into orthogonal bases (\texttt{U\_A},
  \texttt{V\_A}) and singular values (\texttt{S\_A}).
\item
  Similarly for \texttt{B}.
\item
  \texttt{G\_hat\ =\ U\_B.T\ @\ G\ @\ U\_A} transforms the degraded
  image into the \textbf{SVD (frequency-like) domain}.
\item
  \texttt{S\ =\ np.outer(S\_B,\ S\_A)} forms a 2D array of combined
  singular values representing the blur strength across both axes.
\end{itemize}

At this stage, deblurring becomes an \textbf{element-wise filtering
operation} in the transformed domain.

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{3}{\boxspacing}
\begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
\PY{n}{U\PYZus{}A}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{S\PYZus{}A}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{VT\PYZus{}A} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{svd}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{A}\PY{p}{)} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}, full\PYZus{}matrices=False)}
\PY{n}{U\PYZus{}B}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{S\PYZus{}B}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{VT\PYZus{}B} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{svd}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{)} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}, full\PYZus{}matrices=False)}
\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Transform the degraded image into SVD space}
\PY{n}{G\PYZus{}hat} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{U\PYZus{}B}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{T} \PY{o}{@} \PY{n}{G} \PY{o}{@} \PY{n}{U\PYZus{}A}
\PY{n}{S} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{outer}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{S\PYZus{}B}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{S\PYZus{}A}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{G\PYZus{}hat}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{S}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}
\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

            \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=.5pt, pad at break*=1mm, opacityfill=0]
\prompt{Out}{outcolor}{3}{\boxspacing}
\begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
((256, 256), (256, 256))
\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}
        
    \hypertarget{tikhonov-regularization}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Tikhonov
Regularization}}{Tikhonov Regularization}}\label{tikhonov-regularization}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The Tikhonov solution applies frequency-domain filtering in the SVD
  domain with
  \(F_{\text{hat}} = \frac{S \cdot G_{\text{hat}}}{S^2 + \lambda^2}\)
\item
  The parameter \(\lambda\) controls the trade-off between \textbf{noise
  suppression} and \textbf{sharpness}.
\item
  For each \(\lambda\), we reconstruct the restored image and compute
  its \textbf{PSNR} to quantify quality.
\item
  As \(\lambda\) increases, images become smoother but lose detail.
\end{itemize}

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{4}{\boxspacing}
\begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
\PY{n}{λs} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mf}{.05}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mf}{.01}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mf}{.005}\PY{p}{]}

\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{figure}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{figsize}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{20}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{6}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{gray}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{n}{F\PYZus{}hat} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{S}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{G\PYZus{}hat}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{/} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{S}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{S}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{λ}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{λ = }\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}:.03f\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{, PNSR=}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}:.03f\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{format}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{λ}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{psnr}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{F}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{F\PYZus{}hat}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{size}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{20}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{show}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_7_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{truncated-svd-tsvd}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Truncated SVD
(TSVD)}}{Truncated SVD (TSVD)}}\label{truncated-svd-tsvd}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  TSVD simply truncates all singular values smaller than the threshold
  \(\lambda\).
\item
  This acts like a \textbf{low-pass filter}, removing components
  dominated by noise.
\item
  The number of singular values retained (\texttt{np.sum(mask)})
  directly impacts sharpness.
\item
  Larger \(\lambda\) → fewer components → smoother image.
\item
  Smaller \(\lambda\) → sharper image but potentially more noise.
\end{itemize}

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{5}{\boxspacing}
\begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{psnr-evaluation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{PSNR
Evaluation}}{PSNR Evaluation}}\label{psnr-evaluation}}

Throughout the code, PSNR is used to assess reconstruction quality
\(PSNR = 10 \log_{10} \left( \frac{MAX_I^2}{MSE} \right)\), where
\(MSE = \frac{1}{mn}\sum (f - \hat{f})^2\). Higher PSNR indicates better
fidelity to the original image.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

The next table compares and summarizes the above implementation:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.17}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.20}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.39}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.24}}@{}}
\toprule
Concept & Method & Mathematical Idea & Code Implementation \\
\midrule
\endhead
Blur Model & \(G = BFA^T + N\) & 2D convolution via matrices &
\texttt{B\ @\ F\ @\ A.T} \\
Regularization & Tikhonov & \(F = \frac{S G}{S^2 + \lambda^2}\) &
\texttt{(S*G\_hat)/(S*S+λ**2)} \\
Regularization & TSVD & Truncate small singular values &
\texttt{mask\ =\ S\ \textgreater{}\ λ} \\
Stability & λ ↑ → smoothness ↑ & Controls noise--detail balance &
\texttt{for\ λ\ in\ λs:} \\
Quality Measure & PSNR & Fidelity metric & \texttt{psnr(F,\ F\_hat)} \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{note}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Note}}{Note}}\label{note}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Tikhonov Regularization} smoothly attenuates high-frequency
  noise and provides stable reconstructions.
\item
  \textbf{TSVD} acts as a sharp cutoff filter, often preserving more
  detail but risking ringing artifacts.
\item
  As ( \lambda ) increases, both methods produce smoother (but less
  sharp) results.
\item
  In practice, Tikhonov regularization is preferred for continuous noise
  models, while TSVD is suitable when the singular spectrum has a clear
  noise threshold.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{non-blind-deblurring-with-constrained-least-squares-cls-and-iterated-cls-filtering}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{1.1.2 Non-Blind Deblurring with
Constrained Least Squares (CLS) and Iterated CLS
Filtering}}{1.1.2 Non-Blind Deblurring with Constrained Least Squares (CLS) and Iterated CLS Filtering}}\label{non-blind-deblurring-with-constrained-least-squares-cls-and-iterated-cls-filtering}}

Image blurring is a common degradation caused by camera motion,
out-of-focus lenses, or atmospheric turbulence. Given a \textbf{blurred
and noisy observation} \(g(x,y)\), the goal of \textbf{image deblurring}
(or deconvolution) is to reconstruct the \textbf{original image}
\(f(x,y)\). In the \textbf{linear degradation model}, the observation is
given by: \(g(x,y) = (h * f)(x,y) + \eta(x,y)\), where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(h(x,y)\) is the \textbf{point spread function (PSF)},
\item
  \(*\) denotes convolution,
\item
  \(\eta(x,y)\) is additive noise.
\end{itemize}

or, in frequency domain: \(G(u,v) = H(u,v)F(u,v) + N(u,v)\).

Recovering \(f\) from \(g\) is an \textbf{ill-posed inverse problem},
since the PSF suppresses high frequencies and amplifies noise during
inversion.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{least-squares-and-its-limitations}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Least Squares and Its
Limitations}}{Least Squares and Its Limitations}}\label{least-squares-and-its-limitations}}

The \textbf{classical least-squares (LS)} solution minimizes the squared
reconstruction error: \(J(f) = \| g - h * f \|_2^2\). In the Fourier
domain (denoting Fourier transforms with uppercase letters):
\(F_{LS}(u,v) = \frac{G(u,v)}{H(u,v)}\) (this is the classic
\textbf{inverse filter}). However, this approach is highly unstable when
\(H(u,v)\) approaches zero --- noise is amplified severely, resulting in
poor reconstructions.

The \textbf{Wiener filter} (with \emph{Statistical / MMSE Formulation})
aims to correct it by estimating the original image \(F(u,v)\) from
observed \(G(u,v)\), by minimizing the \textbf{expected mean square
error}: \(J = E[|F(u,v) - \hat{F}(u,v)|^2]\):

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Degradation model: \(G = H F + N\)
\item
  Estimate \(F\) as: \(\hat{F} = WG\)
\item
  Goal: minimize the \textbf{mean square error (MSE)}:
  \(J = E[|F - \hat{F}|^2] = E[|F - W G|^2]\)
\item
  Substituting the model and expandinf, we get,
  \(J = E[|F - W(HF + N)|^2] = E[|F - WHF - WN|^2]\)
  \(= E[|F|^2] - W H^* E[|F|^2] - W^* H E[|F|^2] + |W|^2 (|H|^2 E[|F|^2] + E[|N|^2])\)
\item
  Assume \(F\) and \(N\) are uncorrelated. Define:
  \(S_F = E[|F|^2], \quad S_N = E[|N|^2]\), s.t.,
  \(J = S_F - 2 \Re\{W H^* S_F\} + |W|^2 (|H|^2 S_F + S_N)\)
\item
  Minimize w.r.t \(W^*\):
  \(\frac{\partial J}{\partial W^*}= -H^* S_F + W (|H|^2 S_F + S_N) = 0\)
\item
  Solve for \(W\): \(W = \frac{H^* S_F}{|H|^2 S_F + S_N}\), s.t. we
  have:
  \(\boxed{\hat{F}_{W}(u,v) = \frac{H^*(u,v)}{|H(u,v)|^2 + \frac{S_N(u,v)}{S_F(u,v)}}G(u,v)}\)
\item
  It minimizes \(E[|F - \hat{F}|^2] )\) → it's \textbf{least squares
  optimal}.
\item
  Balances \textbf{deblurring} and \textbf{noise suppression}.
\item
  Reduces to \textbf{inverse filter} when \(S_N \to 0\).
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{constrained-least-squares-cls-solution}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Constrained Least Squares (CLS)
Solution}}{Constrained Least Squares (CLS) Solution}}\label{constrained-least-squares-cls-solution}}

To stabilize the inversion, we \textbf{constrain the solution} by
penalizing roughness in the image with a \emph{Deterministic /
Regularization Formulation}. The \textbf{CLS cost function} adds a
\emph{smoothness constraint} via a regularization term:
\(J(f) = \| g - h * f \|_2^2 + \lambda \| C f \|_2^2\), where: * First
term → \textbf{data fidelity} (reconstruction error) * Second term →
\textbf{regularization term} enforcing smoothness via \(C\), often a
Laplacian operator * \(C\) is a \textbf{high-pass (Laplacian) operator}
that penalizes large image gradients, * \(\lambda > 0\) → regularization
parameter controls the trade-off between fidelity and smoothness
(sharpness and noise suppression).

Taking the Derivative (\textbf{Normal Equations}) and setting gradient
of \(J(f)\) to zero:
\(\frac{\partial J(f)}{\partial f} = -2H^T(g - Hf) + 2\lambda C^T(Cf) = 0\)
and after simplification, we have:
\((H^T H + \lambda C^T C) f = H^T g\). Applying 2-D Fourier transform,
the CLS solution in the \textbf{frequency domain} becomes:

\(\boxed{ F_{CLS}(u,v) = \frac{H^*(u,v)}{|H(u,v)|^2 + \gamma |C(u,v)|^2} G(u,v) }\)

where \(C(u,v)\) is now the Fourier transform of the Laplacian operator
(regularization operator). This formulation acts as a \textbf{controlled
inverse filter}: it inverts \(H(u,v)\) while suppressing high-frequency
amplification through the term \(\gamma |C(u,v)|^2\). Refer to the next
figure:

\hypertarget{section}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\protect\includegraphics{images/icls.png}}{}}\label{section}}

\hypertarget{iterated-cls-filtering}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Iterated CLS
Filtering}}{Iterated CLS Filtering}}\label{iterated-cls-filtering}}

The \textbf{Iterated CLS (ICLS)} method refines the CLS solution
iteratively, reducing computational burden by avoiding direct matrix
inversion. At iteration \(k\), the estimate is updated as:
\(F_{k+1}(u, v) = \beta H^*(u, v) G(u, v) + \big(1 - \beta |H(u, v)|^2 - \lambda |C(u, v)|^2 \big) F_k(u, v)\)
where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\beta\) controls the convergence rate,
\item
  \(\lambda\) controls the regularization strength in each iteration,
\item
  \(C(u, v)\) corresponds to the Laplacian operator.
\end{itemize}

This recursion mimics \textbf{gradient descent} in the frequency domain,
approximating the CLS solution after several iterations. The iterative
approach can achieve comparable results to CLS while offering
\textbf{better adaptability and lower memory cost}.

The \textbf{iterative update} corresponds to a gradient-descent style
iteration on the quadratic objective
\(J(F)=\|G-HF\|_2^2+\lambda\|C F\|_2^2\). With step size \(\beta\), a
frequency-domain update becomes
\(F_{k+1}=\beta H^*G + \big(1-\beta|H|^2-\beta\lambda|C|^2\big)F_k\). *
Let's start from the frequency-domain quadratic objective (equivalent to
Tikhonov / CLS):
\(J(F)=\lVert G - H F\rVert_2^2 + \lambda\lVert C F\rVert_2^2\), where
(\(G\),\(H\),\(F\),\(C\)) are the 2-D DFTs of the observed image, blur
kernel (PSF), unknown image and Laplacian operator (or other high-pass),
respectively. * Compute gradient (frequency-by-frequency; because
convolution diagonalizes in the Fourier
basis):\(\nabla_F J = -H^*(G - H F) + \lambda C^* C F\). * A gradient
descent step with step size \(\beta\) gives
\(F_{k+1} = F_k - \beta \nabla_F J = F_k + \beta H^*(G - H F_k) - \beta \lambda C^* C F_k\).
* Rearrange to get the update equation as
\(F_{k+1} = \beta H^* G + \big(1 - \beta |H|^2 - \beta \lambda |C|^2\big) F_k\).
* Setting (\(\lambda\)=0) recovers an \textbf{Iterative Least Squares
(ILS)} or iterative inverse-like scheme:
\(F_{k+1} = \beta H^*G + (1 - \beta |H|^2) F_k\), which is just the
fidelity-only update. Iterated CLS simply adds the (\(\lambda |C|^2\))
term to penalize roughness each step.

This derivation and the use of spectral diagonalization are standard in
deblurring literature.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{demonstration-cls-and-iterated-cls-in-python}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Demonstration: CLS and Iterated CLS
in
Python}}{Demonstration: CLS and Iterated CLS in Python}}\label{demonstration-cls-and-iterated-cls-in-python}}

Below is a practical implementation for \textbf{non-blind deblurring}
using CLS and Iterated CLS filters.

    \PY{n}{F} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros\PYZus{}like}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{dtype}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n+nb}{complex}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{\PYZus{}} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{num\PYZus{}iterations}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{F} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{beta} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{conj}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{Y} \PY{o}{+} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{beta} \PY{o}{*} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{F}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{F}

\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{deblur\PYZus{}image\PYZus{}ICLS}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{beta}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lambd}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{num\PYZus{}iterations}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}

    \PY{n}{F} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros\PYZus{}like}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{dtype}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n+nb}{complex}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{\PYZus{}} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{num\PYZus{}iterations}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{F} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{beta} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{conj}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{Y} \PY{o}{+} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{beta} \PY{o}{*} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{lambd} \PY{o}{*} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{F}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{F}

\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{wiener\PYZus{}filter}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{K}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{0.01}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{Y} \PY{o}{*} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{conj}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{/} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{K}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{cls\PYZus{}filter}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lambd}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{eps}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{1e\PYZhy{}12}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
\PY{+w}{    }\PY{l+s+sd}{\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}Direct (closed\PYZhy{}form) Constrained Least Squares in frequency domain\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{Y} \PY{o}{*} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{conj}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{/} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{lambd} \PY{o}{*} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{eps}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}


\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} \PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{} Demo \PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}}
\PY{n}{image} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{cv2}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imread}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{images/cameraman.png}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{/} \PY{l+m+mf}{255.0}
\PY{n}{psf} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{cv2}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{getGaussianKernel}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{9}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{5}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{kernel} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{psf} \PY{o}{@} \PY{n}{psf}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{T}
\PY{n}{blurred} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{convolve2d}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{image}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{kernel}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{mode}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{same}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{boundary}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{symm}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{blurred} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{blurred} \PY{o}{/} \PY{n}{blurred}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{max}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{noisy} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{clip}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{blurred} \PY{o}{+} \PY{l+m+mf}{0.1} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{random}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{randn}\PY{p}{(}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{blurred}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{cor} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{noisy}

\PY{l+s+sd}{\PYZsq{}\PYZsq{}\PYZsq{}}
\PY{l+s+sd}{image = cv2.imread(\PYZsq{}images/book.png\PYZsq{}, 0).astype(float)}
\PY{l+s+sd}{image /= image.max()}
\PY{l+s+sd}{\PYZsh{} Motion blur kernel (vertical line)}
\PY{l+s+sd}{k = 7}
\PY{l+s+sd}{kernel = np.eye(k) / k}
\PY{l+s+sd}{blurred = convolve2d(image, kernel, mode=\PYZsq{}same\PYZsq{}, boundary=\PYZsq{}symm\PYZsq{})}
\PY{l+s+sd}{blurred /= blurred.max()}
\PY{l+s+sd}{noisy = np.clip(blurred + 0.05 * np.random.randn(*blurred.shape), 0, 1)}
\PY{l+s+sd}{cor = noisy}
\PY{l+s+sd}{\PYZsq{}\PYZsq{}\PYZsq{}}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Fourier transforms}
\PY{n}{Y} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fft}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fft2}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{cor}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{H} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fft}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fft2}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{kernel}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{s}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{image}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{laplacian} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{array}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{p}{[}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{4}\PY{p}{,} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{C} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fft}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fft2}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{laplacian}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{s}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{image}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Parameters}
\PY{n}{beta}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lamd}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{niter}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{K} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mf}{0.15}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mf}{0.5}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{30}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mf}{0.05}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Deblurring}
\PY{n}{F\PYZus{}ILS}  \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{deblur\PYZus{}image\PYZus{}ILS}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{beta}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{niter}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{F\PYZus{}ICLS} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{deblur\PYZus{}image\PYZus{}ICLS}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{beta}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lambd}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{niter}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{F\PYZus{}CLS}  \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{cls\PYZus{}filter}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lambd}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{1.5}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{F\PYZus{}WLS} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{wiener\PYZus{}filter}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{K}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Plot results}
\PY{n}{plot\PYZus{}images}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{image}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{cor}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{F\PYZus{}ILS}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{F\PYZus{}ICLS}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{F\PYZus{}WLS}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{F\PYZus{}CLS}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{beta}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lambd}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{niter}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_11_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{note}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Note}}{Note}}\label{note}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{CLS Filtering} adds a \textbf{smoothness constraint} that
  stabilizes deblurring and reduces ringing artifacts compared to direct
  inverse filtering.
\item
  The \textbf{regularization parameter γ} determines the trade-off:
  small γ → sharper results but higher noise; large γ → smoother images.
\item
  \textbf{Iterated CLS} progressively improves details by combining
  fidelity correction and regularization in an iterative loop.
\end{itemize}

The next table compares Wiener, CLS and iterated CLS filters:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Method} & \textbf{Regularization} & \textbf{Comments} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\textbf{Noisy Input} & --- & Severe blur + noise \\
\textbf{CLS Filter} & Laplacian & Smooth yet stable \\
\textbf{Iterated CLS} & Laplacian + Iteration & Sharper edges, better
contrast \\
\textbf{Wiener Filter} & Noise/Signal ratio & Slight oversmoothing \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

CLS and Iterated CLS can be viewed as \textbf{early optimization-based
methods}, paving the way for \textbf{modern variational and proximal
algorithms} like FISTA and ADMM. The next table summarizes the
differences between CLS and its iterated version:

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lll@{}}
\toprule
\textbf{Aspect} & \textbf{CLS} & \textbf{Iterated CLS} \\
\midrule
\endhead
\textbf{formulation} & Closed-form (Fourier domain) & Iterative
correction-based \\
\textbf{Regularization} & Laplacian constraint & Laplacian + gradient
feedback \\
\textbf{Stability} & High & Very high \\
\textbf{Sharpness} & Moderate & Better \\
\textbf{Computation} & Fast (FFT-based) & Slower, but improves
quality \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

CLS filtering remains one of the \textbf{most practical and efficient}
linear regularization techniques in non-blind image restoration.
\#\#\#\# ✅ Key Insights

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{CLS} smooths aggressively with Laplacian regularization, good
  for Gaussian blur.
\item
  \textbf{Iterated CLS} improves restoration through iterative
  back-projection + regularization --- sharper edges, fewer ringing
  artifacts.
\item
  \textbf{Wiener (Freq)} acts as a simple, fast baseline assuming
  Gaussian noise and known blur kernel.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{blind-deblurring-deconvolution-with-richardson-lucy-algorithm}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{1.2 Blind Deblurring /
Deconvolution with Richardson-Lucy
Algorithm}}{1.2 Blind Deblurring / Deconvolution with Richardson-Lucy Algorithm}}\label{blind-deblurring-deconvolution-with-richardson-lucy-algorithm}}

As usual, we model the observed image \(b(x,y)\) as a convolution of the
true image \(f(x,y)\) with a point-spread function (PSF) \(g(x,y)\),
corrupted by noise: \(b = f * g + n\). When the noise is Poisson (common
for photon-limited imaging), maximum likelihood estimation of \(f\)
given \(b\) and known \(g\) leads to the Richardson--Lucy iteration.

\hypertarget{non-blind-richardsonlucy-known-psf}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Non-blind Richardson--Lucy (known
PSF)}}{Non-blind Richardson--Lucy (known PSF)}}\label{non-blind-richardsonlucy-known-psf}}

The RL multiplicative update for image estimate \(f^{(k)}\) is:
\(f^{(k+1)}(x,y) ;=; f^{(k)}(x,y);\cdot; \bigg[, g^\dagger * \Big(\frac{b}{f^{(k)}*g}\Big)\bigg](x,y)\),
where \(g^\dagger(x,y)=g(-x,-y)\) is the flipped PSF (so convolution
with \(g^\dagger\) is a correlation with \(g\)). The division and
multiplication are pointwise and an additive small constant
\(\varepsilon\) is usually used in denominators to avoid division by
zero.

Intuition:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Compute the \emph{relative blur} \(r = b / (f * g)\).
\item
  Correlate (convolve with flipped kernel) \(r\) with \(g\); that gives
  where \(f\) should be increased/decreased.
\item
  Multiply that correction into the current estimate \(f\).
\end{itemize}

RL is derived from maximizing the Poisson log-likelihood using an
expectation--maximization style derivation; it is non-linear and
multiplicative, preserves nonnegativity, and naturally enforces blur
invariance.

\hypertarget{blind-richardsonlucy-unknown-psf}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Blind Richardson--Lucy (unknown
PSF)}}{Blind Richardson--Lucy (unknown PSF)}}\label{blind-richardsonlucy-unknown-psf}}

When the PSF \(g\) is unknown, a common approach is \textbf{alternating
blind RL}:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Initialize \(f^{(0)}\) and \(g^{(0)}\) (PSF normalized, nonnegative).
\item
  Repeat for \(t=0,1,\dots\):

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Fix \(g^{(t)}\), update image estimate by iterating RL on \(f\):
    \(f^{(t)} \leftarrow f^{(t)} \cdot \Big( g^{(t)\dagger} * \frac{b}{f^{(t)} * g^{(t)} + \varepsilon} \Big)\)
    (one or several inner iterations).
  \item
    Fix \(f^{(t)}\), update PSF estimate by analogous multiplicative
    formula:
    \(g^{(t+1)} \leftarrow g^{(t)} \cdot \text{(correlation of $f^{(t)}$ with } \frac{b}{f^{(t)}*g^{(t)}+\varepsilon}\text{)}\)
    and then \textbf{enforce nonnegativity + normalize}:
    \(g\leftarrow \max(g,0)\); \(g\leftarrow g/\sum g\).
  \end{itemize}
\end{enumerate}

This alternating scheme is heuristic but widely used; it can succeed if:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  PSF support is small and well-initialized,
\item
  Positivity and normalization are enforced,
\item
  Regularization (e.g.~small smoothness prior on PSF or image) is used
  if needed.
\end{itemize}

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{ }{\boxspacing}
\begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

            \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} numerical safeguard: enforce nonnegativity}
            \PY{n}{f} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{clip}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{f}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{k+kc}{None}\PY{p}{)}

        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} \PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{} PSF update (fix f, update g) \PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} conv\PYZus{}fg = f * g  (recompute after f updated)}
        \PY{n}{conv\PYZus{}fg} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{convolve2d}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{f}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{g}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{mode}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{same}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{boundary}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{boundary}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} relative = r = b / (f*g + eps)}

        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} compute full\PYZhy{}size cross\PYZhy{}correlation between r and f:}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} corr(sx,sy) = sum\PYZus{}x,y r(x,y) * f(x+sx, y+sy)}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} this matrix tells how shifting the PSF (at each shift) matches residuals}
        \PY{n}{corr} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{correlate2d}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{relative}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{f}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{mode}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{same}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{boundary}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{boundary}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} extract the central patch (size equals PSF) \PYZhy{}\PYZgt{} multiplicative PSF factor}
        \PY{n}{start\PYZus{}i} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{corr}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{ph}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{/}\PY{o}{/} \PY{l+m+mi}{2}
        \PY{n}{start\PYZus{}j} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{corr}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{pw}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{/}\PY{o}{/} \PY{l+m+mi}{2}
        \PY{n}{corr\PYZus{}center} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{corr}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{start\PYZus{}i}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{start\PYZus{}i} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{ph}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{start\PYZus{}j}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{start\PYZus{}j} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{pw}\PY{p}{]}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} corr\PYZus{}center corresponds to corr evaluated at shifts where PSF support applies}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} multiplicative PSF update:}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} g \PYZlt{}\PYZhy{} g .* corr\PYZus{}center  (elementwise), analogous to image RL step but on kernel support}
        \PY{n}{g} \PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{corr\PYZus{}center}

        \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{psf\PYZus{}clip}\PY{p}{:}
            \PY{n}{g} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{clip}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{g}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{k+kc}{None}\PY{p}{)}  \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} enforce nonnegativity}

        \PY{n}{g\PYZus{}sum} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{g}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{g\PYZus{}sum} \PY{o}{\PYZlt{}}\PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{eps}\PY{p}{:}
            \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} if PSF collapses (sum \PYZti{} 0), reinitialize to uniform small kernel}
            \PY{n}{g} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{ones\PYZus{}like}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{g}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{/} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{g}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{size}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{k}{else}\PY{p}{:}
            \PY{n}{g} \PY{o}{/}\PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{g\PYZus{}sum}           \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} normalize PSF: sum(g)=1}

    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{f}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{g}


\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} load image (grayscale)}
\PY{n}{im} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{io}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imread}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{images/book.png}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{as\PYZus{}gray}\PY{o}{=}\PY{k+kc}{True}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{astype}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{float64}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{im} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{im} \PY{o}{/} \PY{n}{im}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{max}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} true PSF (for simulation)}
\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{gaussian\PYZus{}kernel}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{size}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{5}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{sigma}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{1.0}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
    \PY{n}{ax} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{arange}\PY{p}{(}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{size} \PY{o}{/}\PY{o}{/} \PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{size} \PY{o}{/}\PY{o}{/} \PY{l+m+mi}{2} \PY{o}{+} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{xx}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{yy} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{meshgrid}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{k} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{exp}\PY{p}{(}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{xx}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{yy}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{/} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{2} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{sigma}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{k} \PY{o}{/} \PY{n}{k}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{random}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{seed}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{psf\PYZus{}true} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{gaussian\PYZus{}kernel}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{5}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{blurred} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{convolve2d}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{im}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{psf\PYZus{}true}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{mode}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{same}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{boundary}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{symm}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{noisy} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{blurred} \PY{o}{+} \PY{l+m+mf}{0.01} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{random}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{randn}\PY{p}{(}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{blurred}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} initial guesses}
\PY{n}{f0} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{noisy}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{copy}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{g0} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{random}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{rand}\PY{p}{(}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{psf\PYZus{}true}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{g0} \PY{o}{/}\PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{g0}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{f\PYZus{}rest}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{g\PYZus{}est} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{richardson\PYZus{}lucy\PYZus{}blind}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{noisy}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{f0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{g0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{n\PYZus{}psf\PYZus{}updates}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{10}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{n\PYZus{}image\PYZus{}updates}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{10}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} show}
\PY{n}{fig}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{ax} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{subplots}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{4}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{figsize}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{12}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{3}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imshow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{im}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{cmap}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{gray}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{;} \PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{set\PYZus{}title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Original}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{;} \PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imshow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{noisy}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{cmap}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{gray}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{;} \PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{set\PYZus{}title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Blurred + Noise}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{;} \PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imshow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{clip}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{f\PYZus{}rest}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{cmap}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{gray}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{;} \PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{set\PYZus{}title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Restored}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{;} \PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{3}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imshow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{g\PYZus{}est} \PY{o}{/} \PY{n}{g\PYZus{}est}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{max}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{cmap}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{gray}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{;} \PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{3}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{set\PYZus{}title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Estimated PSF}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{;} \PY{n}{ax}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{3}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{tight\PYZus{}layout}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{;} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{show}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

    \hypertarget{explanation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Explanation}}{Explanation}}\label{explanation}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Image RL update (inner loop)}:
  \(r = \frac{b}{f * g + \varepsilon},\)
  \(f \leftarrow f \cdot \big(g^\dagger * r\big)\). This is the standard
  Richardson--Lucy multiplicative image update derived from the Poisson
  log-likelihood (EM-style).
\item
  \textbf{PSF update (outer step)}:
  \(C(\tau) = \text{corr}(r, f)(\tau),\)
  \(g(\tau) \leftarrow g(\tau)\cdot C_{\mathrm{center}}(\tau)\), where
  \texttt{C\_center} is the central \texttt{ph\ x\ pw} window of the
  correlation. This gives the elementwise multiplicative update for each
  PSF element (each shift), followed by nonnegativity enforcement and
  normalization.
\item
  The function \texttt{richardson\_lucy\_blind()} implements the
  standard \textbf{alternating blind RL} algorithm:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    inner RL: image update with current PSF,
  \item
    outer step: PSF update by cross-correlating residuals with current
    image and extracting the PSF support.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Proper use of \texttt{correlate2d} and \textbf{explicit central patch
  extraction} are essential; they produce a PSF-sized multiplicative
  map.
\item
  It enforces \textbf{positivity} and \textbf{normalization} of the PSF
  (sum=1), removing scale ambiguity and keeping the PSF interpretable.
\end{itemize}

    \hypertarget{iterative-optimization-algorithms-in-image-processing}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{2. Iterative Optimization Algorithms
in Image
Processing}}{2. Iterative Optimization Algorithms in Image Processing}}\label{iterative-optimization-algorithms-in-image-processing}}

Iterative optimization algorithms form the computational backbone of
modern image processing and computer vision. Many fundamental
tasks---such as \textbf{denoising}, \textbf{deblurring},
\textbf{inpainting}, \textbf{super-resolution}, and
\textbf{reconstruction}---can be formulated as minimizing an energy
functional or solving a large-scale inverse problem. Since images are
typically high-dimensional objects (often containing millions of
variables), \textbf{closed-form solutions are rarely feasible}, making
iterative solvers indispensable.

This section presents three cornerstone iterative methods:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Gradient Descent and Steepest Descent}
\item
  \textbf{Conjugate Gradient}
\item
  \textbf{Newton's Method}
\end{enumerate}

All three arise naturally from variational formulations and
least-squares models and differ primarily in how much \textbf{curvature
information} they exploit.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{gradient-descent-and-steepest-descent}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{2.1 Gradient Descent and Steepest
Descent}}{2.1 Gradient Descent and Steepest Descent}}\label{gradient-descent-and-steepest-descent}}

Let \(u \in \mathbb{R}^N\) denote an unknown image (vectorized), and let
\(f\) be the observed noisy image. Many image restoration problems are
formulated as the minimization of an energy functional:
\(E(u) = D(u,f) + \lambda R(u)\), where \(D(u,f)\) is a data-fidelity
term and \(R(u)\) is a regularization functional encoding prior
knowledge. One of the simplest and most widely used optimization methods
is \textbf{gradient descent}, which iteratively updates the image
estimate \(u\) along the direction of the negative gradient of the
energy functional: \(u^{(k+1)} = u^{(k)} - \eta \nabla E(u^{(k)})\),
where \(eta > 0\) is the \textbf{learning rate} or \textbf{step size},
and \(\nabla E(u)\) denotes the gradient of the energy with respect to
the image. For a simple quadratic energy
\(E(u) = \frac{1}{2}|u - f|^2 \quad \Rightarrow \quad \nabla E(u) = u - f\),
the update becomes: \(u^{(k+1)} = u^{(k)} - \eta (u^{(k)} - f)\), which
converges to the trivial solution \(u^\ast = f\). This illustrates that
\textbf{regularization is essential} in ill-posed imaging problems (for
a purely fidelity-driven model).

\hypertarget{regularized-gradient-descent}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Regularized Gradient
Descent}}{Regularized Gradient Descent}}\label{regularized-gradient-descent}}

In practical imaging problems, the observed data \(f\) is often noisy or
incomplete, so regularization plays a crucial role. A widely used
quadratic regularization model is:
\(E(u) = \frac{1}{2}|u - f|^2 + \frac{\lambda}{2}|\nabla u|^2\), where
\(\nabla u\) denotes spatial image gradients. The
\textbf{Euler--Lagrange equation} (refer to the \textbf{variational
methods} chapter) associated with this functional yields:
\(\frac{\partial u}{\partial t} = f - u + \lambda \Delta u\), where
\(\Delta\) is the Laplacian operator. This reveals that gradient descent
corresponds to a \textbf{diffusion process}, smoothing noise while
respecting data fidelity.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{python-demonstration-gradient-descent-and-regularization}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Python Demonstration: Gradient
Descent and
Regularization}}{Python Demonstration: Gradient Descent and Regularization}}\label{python-demonstration-gradient-descent-and-regularization}}

Below is a simple illustration of gradient descent for image restoration
with quadratic regularization.

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
