    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_16_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{explanation}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Explanation}}{Explanation}}\label{explanation}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The observed image \texttt{f\_noisy} is formed by adding Gaussian
  noise to the original.
\item
  The function \texttt{gradient\_descent\_denoising()} minimizes the
  energy functional using iterative gradient descent.
\item
  The \textbf{Laplacian operator} acts as the spatial regularization
  term (enforces smoothness).
\item
  Each iteration updates ( u ) according to:
  \(u^{(k+1)} = u^{(k)} - \eta [(u^{(k)} - f) - \lambda \Delta u^{(k)}]\)
\item
  Each iteration performs an explicit time step of a diffusion PDE.
\item
  The process progressively smooths noise while preserving structural
  features.
\item
  Convergence is slow for ill-conditioned operators, motivating more
  advanced methods.
\end{itemize}

    \hypertarget{conjugate-gradient-method}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{2.2. Conjugate Gradient
Method}}{2.2. Conjugate Gradient Method}}\label{conjugate-gradient-method}}

Many image processing problems---particularly denoising, deblurring, and
regularized reconstruction---lead to the minimization of a
\textbf{quadratic energy functional} of the form:
\(E(u) = \frac{1}{2}u^T A u - b^T u\), with the gradient
\(\nabla E(u) = A u - b\), the minimizer satisfies the linear system:
\(A u = b\), where \(A\) is \textbf{symmetric positive definite (SPD)}.
In imaging, such systems arise from \textbf{Tikhonov regularization},
\textbf{CLS filtering}, and \textbf{diffusion-based denoising}. Gradient
descent converges slowly when \(A\) is ill-conditioned. The
\textbf{Conjugate Gradient (CG)} method overcomes this by generating
\textbf{mutually \(A\)-conjugate search directions}, ensuring that
progress along one direction is never undone. \textbf{CG} constructs a
sequence of estimates \(u^{(k)}\) that minimize \(E(u)\) over
progressively larger \textbf{Krylov subspaces}:
\(\mathcal{K}_k = \text{span}{r_0, A r_0, A^2 r_0, \ldots, A^{k-1} r_0}\),
where \(r_0 = b - A u^{(0)}\) is the initial residual. Each update
minimizes the energy \textbf{exactly along a carefully chosen
direction}, avoiding redundant movement in previously explored
directions.

For quadratic Tikhonov denoising:
\(E(u) = \frac{1}{2}|u - f|^2 + \frac{\lambda}{2}|\nabla u|^2\), the
\textbf{Euler--Lagrange equation} yields:
\((I - \lambda \Delta) u = f\), where \(\Delta\) is the discrete
Laplacian.

Here:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(A = I - \lambda \Delta\)
\item
  \(b = f\)
\end{itemize}

This operator is \textbf{SPD}, making CG an ideal solver.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{algorithm-conjugate-gradient}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Algorithm (Conjugate
Gradient)}}{Algorithm (Conjugate Gradient)}}\label{algorithm-conjugate-gradient}}

Given \(A u = b\):

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Initialize \(u_0\), \(r_0 = b - A u_0\), \(p_0 = r_0\)
\item
  For \(k = 0,1,\dots\): \(\alpha_k = \frac{r_k^T r_k}{p_k^T A p_k}\)
  \(u_{k+1} = u_k + \alpha_k p_k\) \(r_{k+1} = r_k - \alpha_k A p_k\)
  \(\beta_k = \frac{r_{k+1}^T r_{k+1}}{r_k^T r_k}\)
  \(p_{k+1} = r_{k+1} + \beta_k p_k\)
\end{enumerate}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

The next python code snippet implements the \textbf{CG} algorithm and
denoises the same cameraman noisy image.

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{39}{\boxspacing}
\begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
\PY{k+kn}{import}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nn}{numpy}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{k}{as}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nn}{np}
\PY{k+kn}{import}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nn}{cv2}

\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{laplacian}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{u}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{p}{(}
        \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{4} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{u}

    \PY{p}{)}

\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{A}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{u}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lam}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
\PY{+w}{    }\PY{l+s+sd}{\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}Linear operator: (I \PYZhy{} λΔ)u\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{lam} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{laplacian}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{u}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{conjugate\PYZus{}gradient}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{f}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lam}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{0.2}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{max\PYZus{}iter}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{500}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{tol}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{1e\PYZhy{}6}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
    \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Initial guess}
    \PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{f}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{copy}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{n}{r} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{f} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{A}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{u}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lam}\PY{p}{)}          \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Residual}
    \PY{n}{p} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{r}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{copy}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}               \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Search direction}
    \PY{n}{rs\PYZus{}old} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{r} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{r}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{\PYZus{}} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{max\PYZus{}iter}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{Ap} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{A}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{p}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lam}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{alpha} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{rs\PYZus{}old} \PY{o}{/} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{p} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{Ap}\PY{p}{)}

        \PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{alpha} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{p}
        \PY{n}{r} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{r} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{alpha} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{Ap}

        \PY{n}{rs\PYZus{}new} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{r} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{r}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sqrt}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{rs\PYZus{}new}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{\PYZlt{}} \PY{n}{tol}\PY{p}{:}
            \PY{k}{break}

        \PY{n}{p} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{r} \PY{o}{+} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{rs\PYZus{}new} \PY{o}{/} \PY{n}{rs\PYZus{}old}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{p}
        \PY{n}{rs\PYZus{}old} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{rs\PYZus{}new}

    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{u}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Load and normalize test image}
\PY{n}{f} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{img\PYZus{}as\PYZus{}float}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{data}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{camera}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}  \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} grayscale 512x512 image}

\PY{n}{f\PYZus{}denoised} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{conjugate\PYZus{}gradient}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{f\PYZus{}noisy}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lam}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{10}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{figure}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{figsize}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{11}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{4}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_19_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \textbf{Key Insight}

Conjugate Gradient is the \textbf{workhorse solver} for large-scale
quadratic inverse problems in imaging.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{newtons-method}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{2.3 Newton's
Method}}{2.3 Newton's Method}}\label{newtons-method}}

\hypertarget{second-order-optimization}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Second-Order
Optimization}}{Second-Order Optimization}}\label{second-order-optimization}}

Newton's method incorporates \textbf{second-order curvature information}
via the Hessian:
\(u^{(k+1)} = u^{(k)} - [\nabla^2 E(u^{(k)})]^{-1} \nabla E(u^{(k)})\).
For the quadratic energy:
\(E(u) = \frac{1}{2}|u - f|^2 + \frac{\lambda}{2}|\nabla u|^2\), the
Hessian is constant: \(\nabla^2 E = I - \lambda \Delta\). Thus, Newton's
step solves: \((I - \lambda \Delta) u = f\), which is \textbf{exactly
the same linear system solved by CG}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

The next python code snippet implments \textbf{Newton's Method} via
\textbf{Linear Operators}:

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{40}{\boxspacing}
\begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
\PY{k+kn}{import}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nn}{numpy}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{k}{as}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nn}{np}
\PY{k+kn}{import}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nn}{matplotlib}\PY{n+nn}{.}\PY{n+nn}{pyplot}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{k}{as}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nn}{plt}
\PY{k+kn}{from}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nn}{skimage}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{k+kn}{import} \PY{n}{data}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{img\PYZus{}as\PYZus{}float}
\PY{k+kn}{from}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nn}{scipy}\PY{n+nn}{.}\PY{n+nn}{ndimage}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{k+kn}{import} \PY{n}{laplace}
\PY{k+kn}{from}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nn}{scipy}\PY{n+nn}{.}\PY{n+nn}{sparse}\PY{n+nn}{.}\PY{n+nn}{linalg}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{k+kn}{import} \PY{n}{cg}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{LinearOperator}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Load image}

    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{lam} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{laplace}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{u}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{newton\PYZus{}denoising}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{f}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lam}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{0.1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
\PY{+w}{    }\PY{l+s+sd}{\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}}
\PY{l+s+sd}{    Newton\PYZsq{}s method for quadratic Tikhonov denoising}
\PY{l+s+sd}{    Solves (I \PYZhy{} λΔ)u = f}
\PY{l+s+sd}{    \PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}\PYZdq{}}
    \PY{n}{shape} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{f}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}
    \PY{n}{N} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{f}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{size}

    \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Initial guess}
    \PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{f}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{copy}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Gradient}
    \PY{n}{grad} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{f}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{lam} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{laplace}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{u}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Define Hessian as LinearOperator}
    \PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{matvec}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{x\PYZus{}img} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{x}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{reshape}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{y} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{A}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x\PYZus{}img}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lam}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{y}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{ravel}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{n}{H} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{LinearOperator}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{N}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{N}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{matvec}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{matvec}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Solve Newton step: H δ = \PYZhy{}∇E}
    \PY{n}{delta}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{info} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{cg}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{,} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{grad}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{ravel}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{maxiter}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{500}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{info} \PY{o}{!=} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n+nb}{print}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{l+s+s2}{Warning: CG did not fully converge}\PY{l+s+s2}{\PYZdq{}}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{delta}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{reshape}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{clip}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{u}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{n}{f\PYZus{}newton} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{newton\PYZus{}denoising}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{f\PYZus{}noisy}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lam}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{figure}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{figsize}\PY{o}{=}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{11}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{4}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{subplot}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{131}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imshow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{f}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{cmap}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{gray}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Original}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{tight\PYZus{}layout}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{show}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

    The next figure shows the above optimization algorithms side-by-side.

\includegraphics{images/iopt.png}

\hypertarget{comparative-summary}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Comparative
Summary}}{Comparative Summary}}\label{comparative-summary}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}lllll@{}}
\toprule
Method & Uses Gradient & Uses Hessian & Convergence & Imaging Role \\
\midrule
\endhead
Gradient Descent & ✔ & ✘ & Slow & Conceptual baseline \\
Conjugate Gradient & ✔ & Implicit & Fast & Practical solver \\
Newton & ✔ & ✔ & Very fast & Theoretical optimum \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{key-takeaway}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Key
Takeaway}}{Key Takeaway}}\label{key-takeaway}}

Iterative optimization algorithms form a hierarchy: \textbf{Gradient
Descent} provides intuition, \textbf{Conjugate Gradient} delivers
efficiency, and \textbf{Newton's method} offers theoretical optimality.
Together, they form the computational foundation of variational and
optimization-based image processing.

Below is a \textbf{book-ready section} that can be inserted naturally
into your \textbf{Optimization Methods in Image Processing} chapter,
immediately \textbf{after gradient-based and second-order methods}, as a
representative example of \textbf{convex, non-smooth variational
optimization} solved with modern numerical solvers.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{linear-programming-methods-for-image-denoising-and-total-variation-minimization}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{2.4 Linear Programming Methods
for Image Denoising and Total Variation
Minimization}}{2.4 Linear Programming Methods for Image Denoising and Total Variation Minimization}}\label{linear-programming-methods-for-image-denoising-and-total-variation-minimization}}

Many image restoration problems can be formulated as convex optimization
problems involving \textbf{nonsmooth objective functions}, particularly
those based on the \(\ell_1\) norm and \textbf{total variation (TV)}
regularization. While such models are often solved using proximal or
splitting algorithms in practice, they can also be expressed
\textbf{exactly} as \textbf{linear programs (LPs)}. This formulation
provides valuable theoretical insight into the geometry, convexity, and
global optimality of imaging inverse problems. In this section, we
demonstrate how \textbf{linear programming} can be used to solve a
classical image denoising problem and discuss its strengths and
limitations in the context of modern image processing.

\hypertarget{l1-based-image-denoising-as-an-inverse-problem}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{L1-Based Image Denoising as an
Inverse
Problem}}{L1-Based Image Denoising as an Inverse Problem}}\label{l1-based-image-denoising-as-an-inverse-problem}}

Let \(g \in \mathbb{R}^N\) denote a noisy image (or image row), and
\(f \in \mathbb{R}^N\) the unknown clean image. A robust data fidelity
model is obtained by minimizing the \(\ell_1\) norm:
\(\min_f ; | f - g |_1\), which is less sensitive to outliers than the
squared \(\ell_2\) norm. However, this problem alone has the trivial
solution \(f = g\), and therefore \textbf{cannot denoise} the image. To
obtain meaningful restoration, we must introduce \textbf{regularization}
that couples neighboring pixels.

\hypertarget{total-variation-regularization-and-the-fused-lasso}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Total Variation Regularization and
the Fused
Lasso}}{Total Variation Regularization and the Fused Lasso}}\label{total-variation-regularization-and-the-fused-lasso}}

A widely used regularizer in imaging is \textbf{total variation (TV)},
which penalizes the sum of absolute intensity differences:
\(\mathrm{TV}(f) = \sum\limits_{i=1}^{N-1} | f_{i+1} - f_i |\).
Combining robust data fidelity with TV regularization yields the 1D TV
denoising problem:
\(\boxed{ \underset{f}{\min} \| f - g \|_1 + \lambda \sum\limits_{i=1}^{N-1} | f_{i+1} - f_i |}\).
This model is also known as the \textbf{fused Lasso} and promotes
\textbf{piecewise smooth} solutions while preserving sharp edges.

\hypertarget{exact-linear-programming-reformulation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Exact Linear Programming
Reformulation}}{Exact Linear Programming Reformulation}}\label{exact-linear-programming-reformulation}}

The problem above is convex but non-differentiable. To convert it into a
linear program, we introduce auxiliary variables:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(u_i \ge |f_i - g_i|\) (\textbf{data fidelity})
\item
  \(v_i \ge |f_{i+1} - f_i|\) (\textbf{TV regularization})
\end{itemize}

The optimization problem becomes:

\[\begin{aligned}
\min_{f,u,v} \quad &
\sum_{i=1}^N u_i + \lambda \sum_{i=1}^{N-1} v_i \\
\text{subject to} \quad
& f_i - g_i \le u_i, \\
& g_i - f_i \le u_i, \\
& f_{i+1} - f_i \le v_i, \\
& f_i - f_{i+1} \le v_i, \\
& u_i \ge 0,; v_i \ge 0.
\end{aligned}\]

This is a \textbf{standard linear program}:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Linear objective
\item
  Linear constraints
\item
  Convex feasible set
\item
  Guaranteed global optimum
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{python-demonstration-lp-based-tv-denoising}{%
\subsubsection{Python Demonstration: LP-Based TV
Denoising}\label{python-demonstration-lp-based-tv-denoising}}

Below is a practical implementation for denoising a real image
\textbf{row-by-row}, which keeps the LP size manageable while clearly
illustrating the method.

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{16}{\boxspacing}

\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{show}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}

\end{tcolorbox}

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_23_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{discussion-strengths-and-limitations}{%
\subsubsection{Discussion: Strengths and
Limitations}\label{discussion-strengths-and-limitations}}

\textbf{Advantages}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Exact convex formulation
\item
  Guaranteed global optimum
\item
  Clear theoretical interpretation
\item
  Direct link to sparsity and TV models
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Limitations}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Poor scalability to large 2D images
\item
  Memory-intensive
\item
  Slower than first-order methods
\end{itemize}

As a result, LP formulations are mainly used for \textbf{theoretical
analysis and small-scale problems}, while practical systems rely on
\textbf{proximal algorithms}, \textbf{ADMM}, or \textbf{primal--dual
methods}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{key-takeaway}{%
\subsubsection{Key Takeaway}\label{key-takeaway}}

\begin{quote}
Linear programming provides a principled and exact formulation of L1-
and TV-based image restoration problems. While not computationally
optimal for large-scale imaging, LP-based models play a crucial role in
understanding convexity, robustness, and global optimality in
optimization-based image processing.
\end{quote}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{total-variation-inpainting-via-convex-optimization}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{2.5 Total Variation Inpainting
via Convex
Optimization}}{2.5 Total Variation Inpainting via Convex Optimization}}\label{total-variation-inpainting-via-convex-optimization}}

Many image restoration problems---such as \textbf{inpainting},
\textbf{denoising}, and \textbf{deblurring}---can be formulated as the
minimization of an energy functional consisting of a \textbf{data
fidelity term} and a \textbf{regularization term}. While quadratic
regularization leads to smooth (often overly blurred) solutions, real
images are characterized by \textbf{piecewise-smooth regions separated
by sharp edges}. To better preserve edges while filling in missing data,
\textbf{Total Variation (TV) regularization} has become a cornerstone of
modern variational image processing. In this section, we study
\textbf{TV-based image inpainting} and demonstrate how it can be solved
elegantly using \textbf{convex optimization} with \textbf{CVXPY},
complementing the iterative methods discussed earlier (Gradient Descent,
Conjugate Gradient, and Newton's Method).

Let \(u \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times n}\) denote the unknown grayscale image
to be reconstructed, and let \(f \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times n}\) be the
observed (corrupted) image. Define the \textbf{known pixel set}:
\(\Omega \subset {1,\dots,m} \times {1,\dots,n}\) which contains indices
of pixels that are \textbf{not corrupted}. We introduce a binary mask:
\(K_{ij} = \begin{cases} 1, & (i,j) \in \Omega \
0, & (i,j) \notin \Omega \end{cases}\). The reconstruction must satisfy:
\(u_{ij} = f_{ij}, \quad \forall (i,j) \in \Omega\).

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{total-variation-regularization}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Total Variation
Regularization}}{Total Variation Regularization}}\label{total-variation-regularization}}

The \textbf{discrete isotropic total variation} of an image \(u\) is
defined as:
\(\mathrm{TV}(u) = \sum_{i,j} \sqrt{ \left(u_{i+1,j} - u_{i,j}\right)^2 + \left(u_{i,j+1} - u_{i,j}\right)^2 }\)

\textbf{Key properties}:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The gradient magnitude is \textbf{not squared}
\item
  Encourages \textbf{sparse gradients}
\item
  Preserves \textbf{edges and discontinuities}
\item
  Produces \textbf{piecewise-constant} solutions
\end{itemize}

This makes TV ideal for inpainting, where the goal is to smoothly
propagate structures across missing regions without blurring edges.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{tv-inpainting-as-a-convex-optimization-problem}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{TV Inpainting as a Convex
Optimization
Problem}}{TV Inpainting as a Convex Optimization Problem}}\label{tv-inpainting-as-a-convex-optimization-problem}}

The TV inpainting problem is formulated as:

\[\begin{aligned}
\min_{u \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times n}} \quad
& \mathrm{TV}(u) \\
\text{subject to} \quad
& u_{ij} = f_{ij}, \quad \forall (i,j) \in \Omega
\end{aligned}\]

\hypertarget{important-observations}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Important
observations}}{Important observations}}\label{important-observations}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The objective is \textbf{convex but non-smooth}
\item
  The constraints are \textbf{linear}
\item
  A \textbf{global optimum} is guaranteed
\item
  No step-size tuning or initialization issues
\end{itemize}

This problem lies outside the scope of classical gradient descent or
Newton's method, and instead is best handled by \textbf{modern convex
solvers}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\textbf{Convex programming} refers to a class of optimization problems
in which the objective function is convex, the feasible set is convex,
and all constraints are convex (typically linear or affine). Formally, a
convex program can be written as
\(\min_{u \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times n}} ; f(u) \quad \text{subject to} \quad g_i(u) \le 0,; h_j(u)=0\),
where \(f\) and \(g_i\) are convex functions and \(h_j\) are affine. A
key theoretical guarantee is that \textbf{every local minimizer is a
global minimizer}, which is crucial in image processing problems where
non-convexity can lead to unstable or ambiguous solutions. In the TV
inpainting problem, the objective \(\mathrm{TV}(u)\) is convex (though
non-smooth), and the pixel-consistency constraints
\(K \odot u = K \odot f\) are linear, making the problem a well-posed
convex program with a unique global optimum.

\hypertarget{implementation-with-cvxpy}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Implementation with
CVXPY}}{Implementation with CVXPY}}\label{implementation-with-cvxpy}}

CVXPY provides a high-level modeling interface for convex optimization
and includes a built-in total variation operator. In the next code
snippet, \textbf{CVXPY} acts as a \emph{modeling layer} rather than a
solver itself. When we write
\(\underset{u}{\min} \mathrm{TV}(u) \quad \text{s.t.} \quad K \odot u = K \odot f\),
CVXPY first verifies that the problem obeys \textbf{Disciplined Convex
Programming (DCP)} rules, ensuring convexity. It then converts the
problem into a standard \textbf{cone program} (typically a second-order
cone program for TV regularization) and passes it to a numerical solver
such as \textbf{SCS}. The SCS (\textbf{Splitting Conic Solver}) uses a
\textbf{first-order operator-splitting method} to solve the
\textbf{primal--dual optimality} (\textbf{KKT}) conditions:
\(0 \in \partial \mathrm{TV}(u) + K^\top \lambda, \quad K \odot u = K \odot f\),
iteratively updating primal and dual variables until convergence. This
approach efficiently handles the non-smooth TV term and large-scale
image variables, producing a globally optimal in-painted image without
step-size tuning or manual iteration design.

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{8}{\boxspacing}
\begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    \hypertarget{interpretation-and-insights}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Interpretation and
Insights}}{Interpretation and Insights}}\label{interpretation-and-insights}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The solution \textbf{exactly matches known pixels}
\item
  Missing regions are filled by minimizing gradient magnitude
\item
  Edges propagate naturally across gaps
\item
  The method avoids oversmoothing common in quadratic regularization
\end{itemize}

Unlike gradient descent--based approaches, this formulation:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Requires \textbf{no learning rate}
\item
  Has \textbf{guaranteed convergence}
\item
  Handles \textbf{non-smooth regularization naturally}
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{relation-to-earlier-optimization-methods}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Relation to Earlier Optimization
Methods}}{Relation to Earlier Optimization Methods}}\label{relation-to-earlier-optimization-methods}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.19}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.21}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.16}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 6\tabcolsep) * \real{0.45}}@{}}
\toprule
Method & Energy Type & Smoothness & Solver \\
\midrule
\endhead
Gradient Descent & Smooth & Quadratic & Iterative \\
Conjugate Gradient & Quadratic & Smooth & Krylov \\
Newton's Method & Twice differentiable & Smooth & Linear solves \\
\textbf{TV Inpainting} & Convex, non-smooth & Edge-preserving &
Interior-point / first-order convex solvers \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

TV inpainting demonstrates how \textbf{variational modeling},
\textbf{convex analysis}, and \textbf{numerical optimization} come
together to solve ill-posed imaging problems robustly.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{proximal-optimization-methods}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{3. Proximal Optimization
Methods}}{3. Proximal Optimization Methods}}\label{proximal-optimization-methods}}

Proximal optimization methods extend classical gradient-based techniques
to handle non-smooth regularization terms that commonly arise in image
processing and inverse problems. By decomposing complex objectives into
simple proximal updates, they enable efficient and scalable solutions
for denoising, inpainting, and sparse reconstruction tasks.

\hypertarget{image-denoising-with-proximal-gradient-methods-lasso-and-tv-priors}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{3.1 Image Denoising with Proximal
Gradient Methods --- Lasso and TV
priors}}{3.1 Image Denoising with Proximal Gradient Methods --- Lasso and TV priors}}\label{image-denoising-with-proximal-gradient-methods-lasso-and-tv-priors}}

This section explains \emph{image denoising} through the lens of
\textbf{optimization}. We show two widely used priors and their
proximal/gradient-based algorithms:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{ℓ₁ (Lasso) sparsity prior} --- implemented as
  soft-thresholding in an orthogonal transform domain (wavelet / DCT).
\item
  \textbf{Total Variation (TV) prior (isotropic)} --- implemented with a
  dual/primal gradient-projection algorithm.
\end{enumerate}

Before the algorithms, let's understand \emph{image prior}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{what-is-an-image-prior}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{What is an image
prior}?}{What is an image prior?}}\label{what-is-an-image-prior}}

An \textbf{image prior} is a mathematical model that encodes what
``natural'' images look like. In inverse problems (denoising,
deblurring, inpainting, super-resolution) the forward model makes
recovery ill-posed: infinitely many candidate images explain noisy
measurements. A prior chooses the \emph{plausible} ones.

Common priors:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Smoothness / Tikhonov (ℓ₂ on gradients):} penalizes large
  gradients, yields overly smooth images.
\item
  \textbf{Sparsity in a transform basis (ℓ₁ on transform coefficients):}
  assumes the true image has a sparse representation in a basis
  (wavelets, DCT); promotes sparse coefficients so edges/texture are
  preserved.
\item
  \textbf{Total Variation (TV):} penalizes the \(ℓ₁\) norm of the
  gradient magnitude (isotropic TV uses \(ℓ_2\) norm, i.e.,
  \(\sqrt{u_x^2+u_y^2}\;\)), which preserves sharp edges while removing
  small oscillations.
\end{itemize}

Regularized denoising is typically written as
\(\underset{x}{\min} \frac{1}{2}|x - b|_2^2 + \lambda R(x)\), where
\(b\) is the noisy image, \(x\) the desired clean image, \(R\) the prior
(regularizer), and \(\lambda>0\) controls strength of the prior.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{l1-regularization-lasso-in-transform-domain-soft-thresholding}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{3.1.1 L1 regularization (Lasso) in
transform domain
(soft-thresholding)}}{3.1.1 L1 regularization (Lasso) in transform domain (soft-thresholding)}}\label{l1-regularization-lasso-in-transform-domain-soft-thresholding}}

Assume image \(x\) is sparse in an orthonormal transform \(T\) (e.g.,
DCT or orthonormal wavelet). Let \(\alpha = T(x)\). Given noisy image
\(b\) (we can equivalently work with transform of data \(y=T(b)\) if
\(T\) is orthonormal), the ℓ₁-regularized problem becomes
\(\underset{\alpha}{min} \tfrac12 \| \alpha - y \|_2^2 + \lambda \|\alpha\|_1\).
This is a classical \textbf{proximity problem} whose solution is the
\textbf{soft-threshold operator} applied elementwise:
\(\hat{\alpha} = \operatorname{soft}(y, \tau), \text{ with } \operatorname{soft}(t,\tau)=\operatorname{sign}(t)\max(0,|t|-\tau)\),
with threshold \(\tau=\lambda/2\) in the way your demo uses (different
scalings appear depending on conventions). Recover \(x\) by inverse
transform \(x = T^{-1}(\hat\alpha)\). This corresponds to one-step
proximal gradient / ISTA with step size 1 (because the data fidelity is
identity in transform domain), as explained in the next figure.

\includegraphics{images/proximal.png}

\hypertarget{demonstration-code-wavelet-and-dct-soft-thresholding}{%
\paragraph{Demonstration code (wavelet and DCT

Below is the demo code you gave, cleaned up and with small fixes
(definitions for \texttt{dct2}/\texttt{idct2}, correct PSNR calls,
correct wavelet coeff packing/unpacking). Run this cell to reproduce the
wavelet/DCT soft-threshold denoising example.

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_27_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{explanation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Explanation}}{Explanation}}\label{explanation}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{dct2} / \texttt{idct2} use orthonormal DCT (type-II with
  \texttt{norm=\textquotesingle{}ortho\textquotesingle{}}) so transform
  is orthonormal; soft-thresholding is valid as a proximal for the ℓ₁
  when transform is orthonormal.
\item
  \texttt{pywt.wavedec2} gives multilevel wavelet coefficients; we
  flatten with \texttt{pywt.coeffs\_to\_array} and later restore with
  \texttt{array\_to\_coeffs} + \texttt{waverec2}. That preserves full
  wavelet structure.
\item
  Threshold \texttt{tau\ =\ λ/2} comes from the demonstration's scaling.
  In practice threshold selection depends on noise variance and
  normalization. SURE, cross-validation, or noise-level formulas can be
  used.
\item
  Soft-thresholding is the proximal operator of ℓ₁ and corresponds to
  one ISTA step for step-size 1 (or the exact solution of the proximal
  subproblem when fidelity is identity in transform domain).
\item
  Wavelets usually produce sparser (more compressible) representations
  than DCT for natural images, so DWT denoising generally preserves
  edges/textures better. DCT tends to smear edges but is computationally
  simpler.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{with-total-variation-l2-prior-tv-isotropic-with-gradient-projection-dual-formulation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{3.1.2 with Total Variation L2 prior}:
TV (isotropic) with gradient-projection (dual
formulation)}{3.1.2 with Total Variation L2 prior: TV (isotropic) with gradient-projection (dual formulation)}}\label{with-total-variation-l2-prior-tv-isotropic-with-gradient-projection-dual-formulation}}

TV-denoising (\textbf{Rudin--Osher--Fatemi} model) solves
\(\underset{x}{\min} \frac12\|x - b\|_2^2 + \lambda TV(x)\), where
isotropic TV is
\(TV(x) = \sum\limits_{i,j} \sqrt{(D_x x_{i,j})^2 + (D_y x_{i,j})^2}\)
with finite-difference operators \(D_x, D_y\). A very effective way to
solve this convex problem is \textbf{via the dual} variable \(p,q\)
representing the dual field of the gradient; \textbf{Chambolle}'s
algorithm (2004) and gradient-projection methods solve a related dual
problem and recover primal \(x\) via
\(x = \operatorname{proj}_{C}\big( b - \lambda \mathcal{L}(p,q) \big)\),
where \(\mathcal{L}\) is the divergence operator (negative adjoint of
gradient) and projection onto \(C\) can be a clipping operation for box
constraints (here the demo uses identity for no constraints). The next
code snippet implements a gradient-projection (dual) scheme --- closely
related to Chambolle--Pock / Teboulle style dual methods --- using the
operators \texttt{L\_op} (divergence) and \texttt{L\_T\_op} (adjoint
gradient), as explained in the next figure.

\includegraphics{images/goa.png}

\hypertarget{demonstration-code-tv-via-gradient-projection}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Demonstration code (TV via
gradient-projection)}}{Demonstration code (TV via gradient-projection)}}\label{demonstration-code-tv-via-gradient-projection}}

Below is the cleaned and self-contained implementation of TV demo:

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{290}{\boxspacing}
\begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} http://www.math.tau.ac.il/\PYZti{}teboulle/papers/tlv.pdf}
\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{L\PYZus{}op}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{p}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{q}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
    \PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{n} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{q}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{p}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}
    \PY{n}{L\PYZus{}pq} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{n}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{p} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{vstack}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{p}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{p} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{hstack}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{p}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{p}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}

             \PY{l+s+sa}{r}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Dual: \PYZdl{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{underset}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZob{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{(p,q)}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{in }\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{mathcal}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}P\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}max\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|P\PYZus{}C(b\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{lambda }\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{mathcal}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}L\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{(p,q))\PYZhy{}(b\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{lambda }\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{mathcal}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}L\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{(p,q))}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|\PYZus{}F\PYZca{}2\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|b\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{lambda }\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{mathcal}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}L\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{(p,q)}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|\PYZus{}F\PYZca{}2\PYZdl{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{size}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{20}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{show}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
Solving with denoise GP
    \end{Verbatim}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
C:\textbackslash{}Users\textbackslash{}Sandipan.Dey\textbackslash{}anaconda3\textbackslash{}lib\textbackslash{}site-packages\textbackslash{}ipykernel\_launcher.py:63:
RuntimeWarning: divide by zero encountered in log
    \end{Verbatim}

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_29_2.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{explanation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Explanation}}{Explanation}}\label{explanation}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \texttt{L\_op(p,q)} computes the \textbf{discrete divergence} of a
  dual field \(p,q\) and returns a scalar field with the same size as
  the image. Implementation pads \texttt{p} and \texttt{q} to compute
  boundary contributions correctly. The expression
  \((\mathcal{L}p,q)_{i,j} = p_{i,j} + q_{i,j} - p_{i-1,j} - q_{i,j-1}\)
  is a finite-difference divergence.
\item
  \texttt{L\_T\_op(X)} computes the \textbf{adjoint} (negative gradient)
  of divergence; it returns \texttt{(p,q)} where
  \(p_{i,j} = X_{i,j} - X_{i+1,j},\quad q_{i,j} = X_{i,j} - X_{i,j+1}\).
\item
  The algorithm maintains dual variables \texttt{p,q} and performs a
  projected gradient step onto the constraint set
  \({(p,q): \| (p_{i,j},q_{i,j})\|_2 \le 1}\). \texttt{projection\_pq}
  enforces this isotropic constraint (Euclidean norm per location
  \(≤ 1\)).
\item
  The iterative structure updates duals, computes a projected primal
  \texttt{proj\_b}, and monitors convergence via relative change.
\item
  After convergence, the primal optimum is recovered via
  \(X^\ast = \operatorname{proj}_C(b - \lambda \mathcal{L}(p,q))\).
\item
  The dual formulation is attractive because the projection per pixel
  onto the unit ball is simple and efficient.
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{practical-notes-and-parameter-tuning}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Practical notes and parameter
tuning}}{Practical notes and parameter tuning}}\label{practical-notes-and-parameter-tuning}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{λ} (lambda) trades off data fidelity vs regularization: larger
  λ → stronger smoothing, edges may be over-smoothed. Choose based on
  noise level (higher noise → larger λ).
\item
  Stopping criteria: relative change in primal (or dual),
  \textbar\textbar primal\_change\textbar\textbar/\textbar\textbar primal\textbar\textbar{}
  \textless{} tol, or fixed iterations.
\item
  Operator implementations must care for boundary conditions; this
  implementation uses zero-padding style implicit boundaries.
  Alternative choices (Neumann/reflecting) can reduce edge artifacts.
\item
  Chambolle's algorithm (2004) offers a specialized efficient solver for
  TV denoising; Chambolle--Pock (2011) generalizes to many problems and
  converges quickly. The gradient-projection algorithm shown is in the
  same family and works well in practice.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{comparison-lasso-transform-ux2113ux2081-vs-tv}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Comparison: Lasso (transform ℓ₁) vs
TV}}{Comparison: Lasso (transform ℓ₁) vs TV}}\label{comparison-lasso-transform-ux2113ux2081-vs-tv}}

\begin{longtable}[]{@{}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.08}}
  >{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.49}}
  >{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{(\columnwidth - 4\tabcolsep) * \real{0.43}}@{}}
\toprule
Aspect & ℓ₁ in transform (wavelet/DCT) & Total Variation (TV) \\
\midrule
\endhead
Preserves & sparse coefficients → textures + multi-scale edges
(wavelets) & sharp piecewise-constant edges \\
Tends to & keep fine texture (wavelet shrinkage) & remove oscillatory
texture; preserve strong edges \\
Solver & soft-thresholding (very cheap) or ISTA/FISTA & dual methods
(Chambolle), primal-dual (Chambolle--Pock) \\
Complexity & O(n) per transform + threshold & iterative (per-iteration
O(n)) \\
Good when & image compressible in transform basis & images are piecewise
smooth with sharp edges \\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

Often the best practical denoiser combines ideas: wavelet shrinkage for
texture and TV for edges, or uses more advanced proximal combinations
(e.g., plug-and-play priors).

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{solving-inverse-problems-in-imaging-with-ista-fista-and-admm-algorithms}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{3.2 Solving Inverse Problems in
Imaging with ISTA, FISTA, and ADMM
Algorithms}}{3.2 Solving Inverse Problems in Imaging with ISTA, FISTA, and ADMM Algorithms}}\label{solving-inverse-problems-in-imaging-with-ista-fista-and-admm-algorithms}}

Inverse problems are central to image restoration and reconstruction
tasks where the goal is to recover an unknown clean image \(x\) from a
degraded observation \(y\). The degradation is typically modeled as:
\(y = Hx + n\), where \(H\) represents a blurring or measurement
operator, and \(n\) is additive noise. Recovering \(x\) from \(y\) is
ill-posed because \(H\) is often ill-conditioned or non-invertible. To
stabilize the inversion, \textbf{regularization} is introduced, leading
to an optimization problem of the form:
\(\underset{x}{\min} \frac{1}{2} \|Hx - y\|_2^2 + \lambda \|x\|_1\).
Here, the first term enforces data fidelity, while the \(\ell_1\)-norm
regularizer promotes sparsity (for example, in a wavelet or gradient
domain).

To efficiently solve this problem, we use \textbf{iterative proximal
algorithms} --- specifically \textbf{ISTA}, \textbf{FISTA}, and
\textbf{ADMM} --- each offering a balance between convergence speed,
stability, and computational complexity.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{iterative-shrinkage-thresholding-algorithm-ista}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Iterative Shrinkage-Thresholding
Algorithm
(ISTA)}}{Iterative Shrinkage-Thresholding Algorithm (ISTA)}}\label{iterative-shrinkage-thresholding-algorithm-ista}}

ISTA performs a simple gradient-descent update followed by a
\textbf{soft-thresholding (proximal)} step to enforce sparsity. It
iteratively refines the solution as:
\(x^{(k+1)} = \text{prox}_{\lambda/\alpha} \left( x^{(k)} - \frac{1}{\alpha} H^T (Hx^{(k)}-y) \right)\),
where \(\alpha\) is a step-size (Lipschitz constant of \(H^T H\)), and
the \textbf{proximal operator} for the \(\ell_1\)-norm is:
\(\text{prox}_{\tau}(x) = \text{sign}(x)\max(|x| - \tau, 0)\). ISTA is
conceptually simple but converges relatively slowly.

A \textbf{proximal operator} is one of the most important ideas in
modern optimization, but it's surprisingly intuitive if explained the
right way - it is a \emph{smart, gentle step toward minimizing a
function}.

Suppose you want to minimize a function \(f(x)\), but it might be:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{nonsmooth} (like \(|x|\) or total variation),
\item
  \textbf{difficult} to minimize directly,
\item
  or you want to break a large optimization problem into simple pieces.
\end{itemize}

The \emph{proximal operator} of \(f\) is defined as:
\(\text{prox}_{\lambda f}(v)= \underset{x}{\arg\min} \left( f(x) + \frac{1}{2\lambda}|x - v|^2 \right)\).
Think of it like \emph{pulling} the point \(v\) toward a better point
for the function \(f\).

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  You start at some point \(v\).
\item
  You consider all possible new points \(x\).
\item
  You choose the one that:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \textbf{minimizes the objective} \(f(x)\) \emph{but also}
  \item
    \textbf{stays close to your current point} \(v\).
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

The second term \(\frac{1}{2\lambda}|x-v|^2\) is a \emph{spring} that
pulls \(x\) toward \(v\). It can be thought of as a \emph{Soft
projection toward the solution}. A proximal operator behaves like: * a
\textbf{projection} if (f) is a constraint indicator (→ exact projection
onto a set),

but more generally it is:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  a \textbf{softened projection},
\item
  where the degree of softness depends on \(\lambda\).
\end{itemize}

If \(\lambda\) is small → the spring term is strong → you stick close to
\(v\). If \(\lambda\) is large → you move more aggressively to minimize
\(f\).

Another metaphor is a single \emph{denoising} or \emph{shrinkage} step.
This is extremely important in imaging and inverse problems. Refer to
the next figure.

\includegraphics{images/ista.png}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{fast-ista-fista}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Fast ISTA
(FISTA)}}{Fast ISTA (FISTA)}}\label{fast-ista-fista}}

FISTA improves upon ISTA by introducing a \textbf{momentum term}
inspired by \textbf{Nesterov}'s acceleration, achieving a convergence
rate of \(O(1/k^2)\). The updates are:

\(x^{(k+1)} = \text{prox}_{\lambda/\alpha}(y^{(k)} - \frac{1}{\alpha} H^T(Hy^{(k)} - y))\)

\(t_{k+1} = \frac{1+\sqrt{1+4t_k^2}}{2}\)

\(y^{(k+1)} = x^{(k+1)} + \frac{t_k - 1}{t_{k+1}}(x^{(k+1)} - x^{(k)})\)

This makes FISTA much faster in practice for large-scale inverse imaging
tasks. Instead of updating only from the last point, FISTA mixes the
last two iterates to build momentum. Then it performs the proximal step
on \(𝑦^{(𝑘)}\), not \(𝑥^{(𝑘)}\). This lets it \emph{glide} downhill much
faster. ISTA takes slow, memoryless steps; FISTA reuses past movement to
gain acceleration, like sliding downhill with momentum.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{alternating-direction-method-of-multipliers-admm}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Alternating Direction Method of
Multipliers
(ADMM)}}{Alternating Direction Method of Multipliers (ADMM)}}\label{alternating-direction-method-of-multipliers-admm}}

ADMM lets two simpler problems \emph{negotiate} until they agree.
Suppose you want to solve: \(\underset{x}{\min} f(x) + g(x)\), but one
of these is difficult OR you want each handled differently (e.g., one
smooth, one nonsmooth). ADMM splits this into two separate subproblems
by introducing a copy of the variable:
\(\underset{x,z}{\min} f(x) + g(z) \quad \text{subject to } x=z\). Now
imagine:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{\(x\) wants to minimize \(f\)}
\item
  \textbf{\(z\) wants to minimize \(g\)}
\item
  but they must eventually \textbf{agree} (the constraint \(x=z\)).
\end{itemize}

ADMM is a \textbf{negotiation} between the two. Each side solves its own
easy problem, then they adjust to meet in the middle. Each iteration:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{x-step:} Solve a simple version of the problem involving only
  \(f\).
\item
  \textbf{z-step:} Solve a simple version involving only \(g\).
\item
  \textbf{Dual update (u-step):} Adjust the \emph{tension} between
  them---like a referee pulling them \textbf{toward agreement}.
\end{enumerate}

Repeat until \(x\) and \(z\) agree. Because it breaks a hard problem
into \textbf{small, easy, modular pieces}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\textbf{ADMM = Proximal gradient + Lagrange multipliers + communication}

ADMM combines:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  ✔ \textbf{Variable splitting}: Breaks a hard problem into two easier
  ones.
\item
  ✔ \textbf{Proximal operators}: Let each part solve its own preferred
  minimization easily.
\item
  ✔ \textbf{Dual ascent (Lagrange multipliers)}: Enforce
  \emph{agreement} between the two variables.
\item
  ✔ \textbf{Augmented Lagrangian stabilization}: Adds a quadratic
  penalty that prevents oscillation.
\end{itemize}

Together, these ensure:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  stability
\item
  fast convergence
\item
  ability to handle nonsmooth terms
\item
  ability to distribute computation across machines
\end{itemize}

ADMM decomposes the optimization problem into subproblems that are
easier to solve. By introducing an auxiliary variable \(v\), the problem
becomes:
\(\underset{x,v}{\min} \frac{1}{2}\|Hx - y\|_2^2 + \lambda \|v\|_1 \quad \text{s.t.} \quad x = v\;\).
ADMM alternates between solving for \(x\), \(v\), and updating a dual
variable \(u\) using the augmented Lagrangian formulation. Its main
strength is flexibility --- it can easily handle various regularizers
and constraints (e.g., total variation, non-negativity).

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{demonstration-code}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Demonstration
Code}}{Demonstration Code}}\label{demonstration-code}}

\textbf{FISTA}, and \textbf{ADMM} for image deblurring and denoising
using a Gaussian blur kernel.

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]

        \PY{n}{x} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{x} \PY{o}{+} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{o}{/}\PY{n}{alpha}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Ht}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{Ht}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{x} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{prox\PYZus{}l1}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lambd} \PY{o}{/} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{alpha}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{residual} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{lambd}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{residuals}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{append}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{residual}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{residuals}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} \PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{} FISTA Implementation \PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}}
\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{FISTA}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{lambd}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{alpha}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{tol}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{1e\PYZhy{}4}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{max\PYZus{}itr}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{50}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
    \PY{n}{H} \PY{o}{=} \PY{k}{lambda} \PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{:} \PY{n}{convolve2d}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{mode}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{same}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{boundary}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{circular}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{Ht} \PY{o}{=} \PY{k}{lambda} \PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{:} \PY{n}{convolve2d}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{mode}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{same}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{boundary}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{circular}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{x0} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros\PYZus{}like}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{y1} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{x0}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{copy}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{t1} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
    \PY{n}{residuals} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}
    \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{\PYZus{}} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{max\PYZus{}itr}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{x1} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{y1} \PY{o}{+} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{o}{/}\PY{n}{alpha}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Ht}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{Ht}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{x1} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{prox\PYZus{}l1}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lambd} \PY{o}{/} \PY{n}{alpha}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{t2} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sqrt}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{o}{+}\PY{l+m+mi}{4}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{t1}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{/}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}
        \PY{n}{y1} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{x1} \PY{o}{+} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{t1}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{/}\PY{n}{t2}\PY{o}{*}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x1}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{x0}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{x0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{t1} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{x1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{t2}
        \PY{n}{residual} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{H}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{lambd}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{residuals}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{append}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{residual}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{x1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{residuals}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} \PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{} ADMM Implementation \PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}\PYZhy{}}
\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{ADMM}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{lambd}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{rho}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{max\PYZus{}itr}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{50}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{tol}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{1e\PYZhy{}4}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{gamma}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{alpha}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{1.5}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
    \PY{n}{dim} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{y}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}
    \PY{n}{Hty} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{convolve2d}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{mode}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{same}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{boundary}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{circular}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{eigHtH} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fft}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fftn}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{s}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{dim}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}
    \PY{n}{v} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mf}{0.5}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{ones}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{dim}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{x} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{v}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{copy}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{dim}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{residuals} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}
    \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{\PYZus{}} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{max\PYZus{}itr}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{rhs} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fft}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fftn}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{Hty}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{rho}\PY{o}{*}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{v}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{u}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{s}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{dim}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{x} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{real}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{fft}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{ifftn}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{rhs}\PY{o}{/}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{eigHtH}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{rho}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{s}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{dim}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{v\PYZus{}old} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{v}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{copy}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{x\PYZus{}hat} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{alpha}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{x} \PY{o}{+} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{alpha}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{v\PYZus{}old}
        \PY{n}{v} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{prox\PYZus{}l1}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x\PYZus{}hat} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{u}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lambd}\PY{o}{/}\PY{n}{rho}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{u} \PY{o}{+}\PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x\PYZus{}hat} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{v}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{rho} \PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{gamma}
        \PY{n}{residual} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{linalg}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{norm}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{v}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{/} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sqrt}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{prod}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{dim}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{residuals}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{append}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{residual}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{v}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{residuals}

    \hypertarget{discussion-of-results}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Discussion of
Results}}{Discussion of Results}}\label{discussion-of-results}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{ISTA}: Converges slowly but steadily; simple and stable.
\item
  \textbf{FISTA}: Accelerates convergence via Nesterov's momentum,
  achieving better PSNR faster.
\item
  \textbf{ADMM}: Generally provides the most flexible and stable
  recovery, especially when non-smooth regularizers or constraints are
  involved.
\item
  The residual plots confirm faster decay for \textbf{FISTA} and
  consistent convergence for \textbf{ADMM}.
\end{itemize}

    \hypertarget{image-inpainting-with-fista-algorithm}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{3.3 Image Inpainting with FISTA
Algorithm}}{3.3 Image Inpainting with FISTA Algorithm}}\label{image-inpainting-with-fista-algorithm}}

\hypertarget{the-image-inpainting-problem}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{The Image Inpainting
Problem}}{The Image Inpainting Problem}}\label{the-image-inpainting-problem}}

You have an image \(x \in \mathbb{R}^{m \times m}\). You observe only
some pixels at indices \(\Omega\), producing: \(b = P_\Omega(x)\), where
\(\displaystyle P_\Omega : \mathbb{R}^{m \times m} \to \mathbb{R}^{|\Omega|}\)
is the projection that \emph{keeps only sampled pixels}. Image
inpainting refers to the process of restoring missing or corrupted
regions of an image using prior knowledge about natural image
structures. This problem can be formulated as an \textbf{inverse
problem}, where the goal is to recover the complete image \(x\) from a
partially observed set of pixels \(b\), such that:

\[\underset{x}{\min} \frac{1}{2} | P_{\Omega}(x) - b |_2^2 + \lambda R(x)\]

Here:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(P_{\Omega}\) is the \textbf{sampling operator} that selects the
  observed pixels,
\item
  \(R(x)\) is a \textbf{regularization term} enforcing prior structure
  or sparsity,
\item
  \(\lambda\) is a \textbf{regularization weight} controlling the
  trade-off between data fidelity and prior smoothness.
\end{itemize}

Two common priors are used:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Sparse representation prior (L1 norm: sparse wavelet
  coefficients)} --- assumes that the image has a sparse representation
  in the wavelet domain (\$ R(x)=∥Wx∥\_1\$).
\item
  \textbf{Total Variation (TV) prior (spatial smoothness)} --- assumes
  that image intensity varies smoothly except across edges
  (\(R(x)=∥x∥_{TV}\)).
\end{enumerate}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{python-implementation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Python
Implementation}}{Python Implementation}}\label{python-implementation}}

Below is a Python implementation of image inpainting using FISTA with
both \textbf{L1} (wavelet-domain sparsity) and \textbf{Total Variation
(TV)} priors.

\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{tv\PYZus{}norm}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{X}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{opt}\PY{o}{=}\PY{k+kc}{None}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}

    \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{opt} \PY{o}{==} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{iso}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{D} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros\PYZus{}like}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{X}\PY{p}{)}
        \PY{n}{D}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{m} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{P1} \PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*} \PY{l+m+mi}{2}
        \PY{n}{D}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{n} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{D}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{n} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{P2} \PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*} \PY{l+m+mi}{2}
        \PY{n}{tv\PYZus{}out} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sqrt}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{D}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{k}{else}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{tv\PYZus{}out} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{P1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{sum}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{P2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{tv\PYZus{}out}

\PY{k}{def}\PY{+w}{ }\PY{n+nf}{reconstruct\PYZus{}tv}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{image}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{indices}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{λ}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{0.01}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{L}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{maxit}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{200}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}    
    \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Define the overall operator}
    \PY{n}{A} \PY{o}{=} \PY{k}{lambda} \PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{:} \PY{n}{p\PYZus{}Ω}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{indices}\PY{p}{)}  \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} P\PYZus{}Omega}
    \PY{n}{AT} \PY{o}{=} \PY{k}{lambda} \PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{:} \PY{n}{p\PYZus{}Ω\PYZus{}T}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{indices}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{)} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} P\PYZus{}Omega\PYZca{}T}
    \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Generate measurements}
    \PY{n}{b} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{A}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{image}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{fx} \PY{o}{=} \PY{k}{lambda} \PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{:} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{o}{/}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{*}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{linalg}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{norm}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{b} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{A}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}
    \PY{n}{gx} \PY{o}{=} \PY{k}{lambda} \PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{:} \PY{n}{tv\PYZus{}norm}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{proxg} \PY{o}{=} \PY{k}{lambda} \PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{:} \PY{n}{denoise\PYZus{}tv\PYZus{}chambolle}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{reshape}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,}
                                              \PY{n}{weight}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n}{λ} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{eps}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{1e\PYZhy{}5}\PY{p}{,}
                                              \PY{n}{n\PYZus{}iter\PYZus{}max}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{50}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{reshape}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{m}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{gradf} \PY{o}{=} \PY{k}{lambda} \PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{:} \PY{n}{AT}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{A}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{b}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{reshape}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{errors} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{FISTA}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{fx}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{gx}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{gradf}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{proxg}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{m}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{λ}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{L}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{maxit}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{x}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{reshape}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}

\PY{n}{shape} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{256}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{256}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Choose optimization parameters}
\PY{n}{m} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{256}
\PY{n}{rate\PYZus{}noise} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mf}{0.4}
\PY{n}{L} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
\PY{n}{λ} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mf}{0.25}

\PY{n}{image} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{cv2}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{resize}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{cv2}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imread}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{images/cameraman.png}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{image\PYZus{}corrupted}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{mask} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{apply\PYZus{}random\PYZus{}mask}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{image}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{rate\PYZus{}noise}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{indices} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{nonzero}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{mask}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{flatten}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}

\PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Reconstruction with L1 }
\PY{n}{reconstruction\PYZus{}l1} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{reconstruct\PYZus{}l1}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{image}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{indices}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{λ}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{reconstruction\PYZus{}tv} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{reconstruct\PYZus{}tv}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{image}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{indices}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{m}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{λ}\PY{p}{)}

          \PY{l+s+sa}{r}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZdl{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{underset}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZob{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{lambda}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{in }\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{mathcal}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}R\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZca{}p\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}min\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{frac}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}1\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}2\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|b\PYZhy{}P\PYZus{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZob{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Omega\PYZcb{}W\PYZca{}T}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{lambda}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|\PYZus{}2\PYZca{}2+2}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{lambda\PYZus{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZob{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{mathcal}\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}l\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZus{}1\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{lambda}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|\PYZus{}1}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{|}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZbs{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{quad\PYZdl{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{size}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{20}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{subplot}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{144}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imshow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{reconstruction\PYZus{}tv}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{aspect}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{auto}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{title}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{reconstructed (TV) (PSNR: }\PY{l+s+si}{\PYZob{}:.03f\PYZcb{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{)}\PY{l+s+se}{\PYZbs{}n}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{format}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{psnr}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{image}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{reconstruction\PYZus{}tv}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{+} \PYZbs{}

\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{axis}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{off}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
time taken by FISTA: 3.1890368461608887
time taken by FISTA: 8.196538209915161
    \end{Verbatim}

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_35_1.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{forward-operator}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Forward
operator}}{Forward operator}}\label{forward-operator}}

The function \texttt{p\_Ω()} implements: \(P_\Omega(x) = x|_{\Omega}\),
meaning: extract only the known pixels.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{adjoint-operator-transpose}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Adjoint operator
(transpose)}}{Adjoint operator (transpose)}}\label{adjoint-operator-transpose}}

The function \texttt{p\_Ω\_T()} implements:
\(P_\Omega^{T} : \mathbb{R}^{|\Omega|} \rightarrow \mathbb{R}^{m \times m}\).
It takes a vector of observed entries and \textbf{places them back} into
a full image at the correct pixel positions, zero elsewhere. This
operator is the adjoint because:
\(\langle P_\Omega(x), y \rangle = \langle x, P_\Omega^T(y) \rangle\).

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{sparse-reconstruction-formulation-l1-case}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Sparse Reconstruction Formulation (L1
case)}}{Sparse Reconstruction Formulation (L1 case)}}\label{sparse-reconstruction-formulation-l1-case}}

We assume the image is sparse in some transform domain \(W\), typically
a wavelet dictionary: \(x = W^{T}\alpha\). Then the \textbf{inpainting
problem} becomes:
\(\boxed{\alpha^* = \underset{x}{\arg\min} \frac{1}{2}\| b - P_\Omega W^T \alpha \|_2^2 + \lambda \|\alpha\|_1}\)
This is exactly the standard \textbf{Lasso} problem in compressed
sensing.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{the-total-variation-tv-formulation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{The Total Variation (TV)
Formulation}}{The Total Variation (TV) Formulation}}\label{the-total-variation-tv-formulation}}

The alternative prior is \textbf{piecewise-smoothness}, expressed via
TV:

\(\boxed{ x^* = \underset{x}{\arg\min} \frac{1}{2} \| b - P_\Omega x \|_2^2 + \lambda \|x\|_{\text{TV}} }\)

where TV is either anisotropic or isotropic. This is the classic
\textbf{Rudin--Osher--Fatemi (ROF) model} solved by FISTA.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

The \textbf{Fast Iterative Shrinkage-Thresholding Algorithm (FISTA)} is
an accelerated first-order optimization method that efficiently
minimizes objectives of the form \(f(x) + g(x)\), where \(f\) is smooth
and differentiable, and \(g\) is convex but possibly non-smooth (like
\(L_1\) or \(TV\) regularization).

\hypertarget{fista-algorithm}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{FISTA
Algorithm}}{FISTA Algorithm}}\label{fista-algorithm}}

At each iteration \(k\), FISTA performs the following updates:

\(x_{k+1} = \text{prox}_{\alpha g}(y_k - \alpha \nabla f(y_k))\)

\(t_{k+1} = \frac{1 + \sqrt{1 + 4t_k^2}}{2}\)

\(y_{k+1} = x_{k+1} + \frac{t_k - 1}{t_{k+1}}(x_{k+1} - x_k)\)

where \(\alpha = 1/L\) and \(L\) is the \emph{Lipschitz constant} of
\(\nabla f\). This accelerated formulation achieves a convergence rate
of \(O(1/k^2)\), outperforming standard ISTA.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{fista-update-loop}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{FISTA update
loop}}{FISTA update loop}}\label{fista-update-loop}}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{x\_k\_next }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ proxg(y\_k }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ α }\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{ gradf(y\_k), α)}
\NormalTok{t\_k\_next }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{1} \OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ sqrt(}\DecValTok{4}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{t\_k}\OperatorTok{\^{}}\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{+} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{))}\OperatorTok{/}\DecValTok{2}
\NormalTok{y\_k\_next }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ x\_k\_next }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ ((t\_k }\OperatorTok{{-}} \DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{)}\OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{t\_k\_next)}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{(x\_k\_next }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ x\_k)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This is exactly the three-equation update above.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{computing-the-gradient-of-the-data-term}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Computing the gradient of the data
term}}{Computing the gradient of the data term}}\label{computing-the-gradient-of-the-data-term}}

For L1 model:
\(f(\alpha) = \frac{1}{2}\|b - P_\Omega W^T \alpha\|_2^2\). Gradient:
\(\nabla f(\alpha)= W P_\Omega^T \big(P_\Omega W^{T}\alpha - b\big)\).
This is literally: \(W( P_{\Omega}^{T}(P_{\Omega}x - b ) )\).

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{proximal-operator-operatornameprox_alpha-lambda-cdot_1}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Proximal Operator
\(\operatorname{prox}_{\alpha \lambda | \cdot|_1}\)}}{Proximal Operator \textbackslash operatorname\{prox\}\_\{\textbackslash alpha \textbackslash lambda \textbar{} \textbackslash cdot\textbar\_1\}}}\label{proximal-operator-operatornameprox_alpha-lambda-cdot_1}}

For the L1 prior:
\(\operatorname{prox}_{\tau |\cdot|_1}(y) = \text{soft}(y,\tau) = \text{sign}(y)\max(|y|-\tau, 0)\).
For TV prior: prox is \textbf{TV denoising} via Chambolle's algorithm.
This matches: \(\operatorname{prox}_{\tau \lambda TV}(x)\).

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{how-the-whole-pipeline-performs-inpainting}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{How the Whole Pipeline Performs
Inpainting}}{How the Whole Pipeline Performs Inpainting}}\label{how-the-whole-pipeline-performs-inpainting}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Step 1 --- Randomly delete observations. This produces \(\Omega\).
\item
  Step 2 --- Compute \(b = P_\Omega(x)\)
\item
  Step 3 --- Set up the inverse problem

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    L1 case:
    \(\underset{x}{\min} \frac{1}{2}||b - P_\Omega W^T x||_2^2 + \lambda ||x||_1\)
  \item
    TV case:
    \(\underset{x}{\min} \frac{1}{2}||b - P_\Omega x||_2^2 + \lambda TV(x)\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Step 4 --- Solve with FISTA
\item
  Step 5 --- Reconstruct the image
\end{itemize}

The code does image inpainting by solving:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  either a \textbf{sparse wavelet prior} (L1)
\item
  or a \textbf{smoothness prior} (TV)
\end{itemize}

via FISTA.

The key operations are listed in the next table:

\includegraphics{images/prox.png}

This is a \textbf{standard, correct, state-of-the-art implementation} of
sparse and TV-regularized inpainting.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{discussion}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Discussion}}{Discussion}}\label{discussion}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{L1 Reconstruction:} Uses wavelet sparsity to preserve global
  structures and smooth regions. However, it may oversmooth edges due to
  basis representation limits.
\item
  \textbf{TV Reconstruction:} Excels at preserving edges and sharp
  transitions while removing noise, but can cause the ``staircasing''
  effect in flat regions.
\item
  \textbf{FISTA Performance:} Converges rapidly (typically within 200
  iterations) and achieves stable reconstruction with significant PSNR
  improvement.
\end{itemize}

In practice, \textbf{TV-FISTA} tends to outperform \textbf{L1-FISTA} for
natural images with prominent edges, while \textbf{L1-FISTA} performs
better for smoother, texture-like images.

\hypertarget{sparse-optimization-in-imaging}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{4. Sparse Optimization in
Imaging}}{4. Sparse Optimization in Imaging}}\label{sparse-optimization-in-imaging}}

Images are often corrupted by noise, missing regions, or degradation due
to imperfect acquisition, transmission, or storage. Traditional methods
(like Gaussian filtering or interpolation) tend to blur important
details or fail to reconstruct large missing regions accurately. A more
modern and powerful approach relies on \textbf{sparse representations}
--- the idea that image patches can be expressed as a \emph{sparse
linear combination} of a few atoms from an overcomplete
\textbf{dictionary}.

Natural images exhibit strong \textbf{structural redundancy} when
observed locally. When small image patches are represented in a suitable
transform domain, their essential content can often be described using
only a few basis elements, while noise typically spreads across all
dimensions. This observation motivates \textbf{sparse
representation--based image denoising}, where each noisy image patch is
approximated as a sparse linear combination of atoms from a predefined
dictionary. This section focuses on two applications of this principle:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Image Inpainting} (filling missing or masked regions)
\item
  \textbf{Image Denoising} (removing additive noise)
\end{itemize}

The computational core is the \textbf{Orthogonal Matching Pursuit (OMP)}
algorithm, a greedy optimization technique that efficiently finds sparse
coefficients.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{the-l_0-norm-and-sparsity}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{The \(L_0\) norm and
Sparsity}}{The L\_0 norm and Sparsity}}\label{the-l_0-norm-and-sparsity}}

\(L_0\) norm of a vector \(x\) is defines as:
\(\|\mathbf{x}\|_{0} = \#\{i : x_i \neq 0\}\), i.e., \textbf{the count
of non-zero entries}, which directly measures \textbf{how sparse} a
representation is --- fewer nonzeros ⇒ fewer active patches/atoms ⇒ a
simpler, more parsimonious reconstruction, matching the intuition in the
patch-based inpainting context where we want \textbf{the smallest number
of patches to explain the image}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{sparse-coding-proble-sparse-representation-and-dictionary-learning}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Sparse Coding Proble: Sparse
Representation and Dictionary
Learning}}{Sparse Coding Proble: Sparse Representation and Dictionary Learning}}\label{sparse-coding-proble-sparse-representation-and-dictionary-learning}}

Given a signal (or image patch) \(\mathbf{x} \in \mathbb{R}^n\), we
represent it as: \(\mathbf{x} \approx \mathbf{D}\boldsymbol{\alpha}\),
where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\mathbf{D} = [\mathbf{d}_1, \mathbf{d}_2, \ldots, \mathbf{d}_K] \in \mathbb{R}^{n \times K}\)
  is the \textbf{dictionary} containing \(K\) basis atoms (\(K > n\) for
  overcompleteness).
\item
  \(\boldsymbol{\alpha} \in \mathbb{R}^K\) is a \textbf{sparse
  coefficient vector}, with only a few nonzero elements.
\item
  \(\varepsilon\) is an error tolerance (related to noise level).
\end{itemize}

The reconstruction is then achieved by finding the sparsest
\(\boldsymbol{\alpha}\):
\(\underset{\boldsymbol{\alpha}}{\min} \| \boldsymbol{\alpha} \|_0 \quad \text{s.t.} \quad \| \mathbf{x} - \mathbf{D}\boldsymbol{\alpha} \|_2 \leq \epsilon\).
This problem is combinatorial --- exact solution requires checking all
possible subsets of atoms (\emph{NP-hard}), but approximate
\textbf{greedy pursuit algorithms} like \textbf{OMP} are efficient and
practical.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{dictionary-construction}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Dictionary
Construction}}{Dictionary Construction}}\label{dictionary-construction}}

\hypertarget{using-transform-based-dictionaries}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Using Transform-Based
Dictionaries}}{Using Transform-Based Dictionaries}}\label{using-transform-based-dictionaries}}

Instead of learning a dictionary from data (like K-SVD), we can
construct analytical dictionaries from transforms such as:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Discrete Cosine Transform (DCT)}
\item
  \textbf{Haar Wavelet Transform}
\end{itemize}

These transforms provide orthogonal or nearly orthogonal bases that are
compact for representing image patches.

\hypertarget{dct-haar-dictionary-generation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{DCT \& Haar Dictionary
Generation}}{DCT \& Haar Dictionary Generation}}\label{dct-haar-dictionary-generation}}

The next code snippet implements a function
\texttt{dictionary\_from\_transform()} where

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Each column of \(H\) represents one \textbf{basis vector} (atom).
\item
  \texttt{np.kron(H.T,\ H.T)} creates \textbf{2D atoms} suitable for
  image patches (outer product basis).
\end{itemize}

This yields dictionaries like:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(D_{\text{DCT}} = \text{kron}(D_{\text{1D-DCT}}, D_{\text{1D-DCT}})\)
\item
  \(D_{\text{Haar}} = \text{kron}(D_{\text{1D-Haar}}, D_{\text{1D-Haar}})\)
\end{itemize}

Thus, each image patch (e.g., \(8\times 8\) block) can be vectorized and
represented as a sparse linear combination of these atoms.
