        \PY{n}{i} \PY{o}{+}\PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{I}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{vseams}

\PY{n}{im}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{mask} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{mpimg}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imread}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{images/shoes.jpg}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{[}\PY{o}{.}\PY{o}{.}\PY{o}{.}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{l+m+mi}{3}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{mpimg}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{imread}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{images/shoemask.jpg}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{[}\PY{o}{.}\PY{o}{.}\PY{o}{.}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{l+m+mi}{3}\PY{p}{]}

    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{object-protection-and-selective-removal}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Object Protection and Selective
Removal}}{Object Protection and Selective Removal}}\label{object-protection-and-selective-removal}}

\hypertarget{mask-semantics}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Mask
Semantics}}{Mask Semantics}}\label{mask-semantics}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Red mask} → remove (low energy)
\item
  \textbf{Green mask} → protect (high energy)
\end{itemize}

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{M[(red pixels)] }\OperatorTok{=} \OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{99999}
\NormalTok{M[(green pixels)] }\OperatorTok{=} \DecValTok{10}\OperatorTok{**}\DecValTok{9}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This enforces constrained optimization:
\(\underset{\mathcal{S}}{\min} \sum\limits_{(i,j)\in\mathcal{S}} E(i,j) \quad \text{s.t. protected pixels are excluded}\).

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{284}{\boxspacing}

    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{discussion-and-limitations}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Discussion and
Limitations}}{Discussion and Limitations}}\label{discussion-and-limitations}}

\textbf{Advantages}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Content-aware resizing
\item
  Preserves important structures
\item
  Enables object removal without inpainting
\end{itemize}

\textbf{Limitations}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Can introduce artifacts with repeated seam removal
\item
  Struggles with textured regions
\item
  Computationally expensive for large images
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

Seam carving reframes image resizing as an \textbf{energy minimization
problem} over pixel paths. By exploiting dynamic programming and
perceptual energy functions, it enables:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Intelligent image resizing
\item
  Object removal
\item
  Object protection
\item
  Adaptive retargeting
\end{itemize}

This chapter demonstrated both the \textbf{theory} and a
\textbf{complete working implementation}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{texture-synthesis-and-transfer-with-image-quilting}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{6.2 Texture Synthesis and Transfer
with Image
Quilting}}{6.2 Texture Synthesis and Transfer with Image Quilting}}\label{texture-synthesis-and-transfer-with-image-quilting}}

Textures are fundamental visual primitives characterized by repeated
patterns, local stationarity, and stochastic variation. Unlike
structured objects, textures lack explicit global geometry but exhibit
strong \textbf{local statistical regularities}. The challenge in texture
synthesis is to generate a new image that is perceptually
indistinguishable from a given sample texture, while texture transfer
extends this idea by enforcing the appearance of one texture over the
structure of another image. Texture synthesis and transfer are
cornerstone techniques in computer graphics and image processing that
enable the creation of new images by replicating the appearance of an
example texture. The goal is to produce visually plausible patterns that
preserve both the \textbf{local statistics} and \textbf{spatial
coherence} of the source texture.

The pioneering work of \textbf{Efros and Freeman (2001)} introduced
\textbf{Image Quilting}, an algorithm that efficiently generates large,
seamless textures by stitching together small overlapping image patches
from a sample image while minimizing visible seams (with minimum error
boundaries). The method elegantly balances \textbf{local similarity},
\textbf{global coherence}, and \textbf{computational efficiency}.

This section introduces two related concepts:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Texture Synthesis using Image Quilting} -- creating large
  textures from small exemplars.
\item
  \textbf{Texture Transfer} -- applying the synthesized texture to a
  target image, matching both the texture appearance and the target's
  structural pattern.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{texture-as-a-stochastic-process}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Texture as a Stochastic
Process}}{Texture as a Stochastic Process}}\label{texture-as-a-stochastic-process}}

Let a texture image be represented as:
\(\mathbf{I} : \Omega \subset \mathbb{Z}^2 \rightarrow \mathbb{R}^3\).
Textures are often modeled as \textbf{locally stationary random fields},
meaning that for a sufficiently small neighborhood \(\mathcal{N}(p)\),
the joint statistics are approximately invariant under translation:
\(p(\mathbf{I}(\mathcal{N}(p))) \approx p(\mathbf{I}(\mathcal{N}(q))), \; \forall{p, q \in \Omega}\).
Image quilting exploits this assumption by copying local neighborhoods
(patches) directly from the exemplar texture.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{patch-based-texture-synthesis-with-image-quilting}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{6.2.1 Patch-Based Texture
Synthesis with Image
Quilting}}{6.2.1 Patch-Based Texture Synthesis with Image Quilting}}\label{patch-based-texture-synthesis-with-image-quilting}}

Given a small texture exemplar \(I(x, y)\), the task is to generate a
larger texture \(O(x, y)\) that looks statistically and perceptually
similar. Let: * \(I \in \mathbb{R}^{H \times W \times 3}\) be the
\textbf{input texture sample} *
\(O \in \mathbb{R}^{M \times M \times 3}\) be the \textbf{output
synthesized texture} * \(B\) be the \textbf{patch size} * \(o\) be the
\textbf{overlap width}, where \(0 < o < B\)

The goal is to synthesize \(O\) such that: \(O \sim \mathcal{T}(I)\)
i.e., \(O\) has the \textbf{same local texture statistics} as \(I\),
while being larger in spatial extent.

Instead of modeling the texture mathematically (e.g., with Markov Random
Fields), Efros and Freeman proposed a \textbf{non-parametric,
patch-based approach}. The synthesized image is built patch-by-patch,
where each new patch overlaps partially with previously synthesized
regions, and the best-fitting patch is chosen using a
\textbf{sum-of-squared-differences (SSD)} matching criterion.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{patch-representation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Patch
Representation}}{Patch Representation}}\label{patch-representation}}

Given a texture exemplar \(\mathbf{I}\), we extract square patches:
\(\mathbf{P}_k \in \mathbb{R}^{B \times B \times 3}\) where \(B\) is the
patch size. The goal is to generate a larger image \(\mathbf{O}\) by
placing patches such that:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Local appearance matches the source texture
\item
  Adjacent patches blend seamlessly
\end{enumerate}

\hypertarget{patch-dictionary-construction}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Patch Dictionary
Construction}}{Patch Dictionary Construction}}\label{patch-dictionary-construction}}

The algorithm implicitly constructs a \textbf{patch dictionary}:
\(\mathcal{P} = { P_k \mid P_k = I[x_k : x_k + B, y_k : y_k + B] }\)
where each \(P_k \in \mathbb{R}^{B \times B \times 3}\). This defines a
\textbf{non-parametric texture model} --- no learning, only sampling.

\hypertarget{output-lattice-and-placement-geometry}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Output Lattice and Placement
Geometry}}{Output Lattice and Placement Geometry}}\label{output-lattice-and-placement-geometry}}

Let the patch stride be: \(s = B - o\). Patch top-left coordinates are:
\((i,j) \in {0, s, 2s, \dots}\), with a \textbf{forced final placement}
at \((M-B, N-B)\) to ensure full coverage. This resolves boundary
undercoverage (e.g., black or blank borders) that arise from uniform
stepping alone.

\hypertarget{nauxefve-texture-synthesis-baseline}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Naïve Texture Synthesis
(Baseline)}}{Naïve Texture Synthesis (Baseline)}}\label{nauxefve-texture-synthesis-baseline}}

A trivial approach randomly samples patches and tiles them, using the
following line of python code, as shown in the next code snippet:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{rand\_gen[i:i}\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{B, j:j}\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{B] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ samples[random]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

This preserves local statistics but introduces severe blocking artifacts
because \textbf{no boundary consistency} is enforced.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{overlap-constrained-patch-placement}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Overlap-Constrained Patch
Placement}}{Overlap-Constrained Patch Placement}}\label{overlap-constrained-patch-placement}}

\textbf{Overlapping Regions}

To enforce continuity, patches overlap by \(o\) pixels:
\(o = \lfloor \frac{B}{6} \rfloor\). Let:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\mathbf{O}\): current (partially) synthesized (output) image
\item
  \(\mathbf{P}\): candidate patch from the source texture
\item
  \(\Omega\) be the overlap region.
\end{itemize}

When placing a patch at position \((i,j)\), the overlap regions are:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Left overlap} (if \(j > 0\)):
  \(\Omega_L = O[i : i+B,; j : j+o]\)
\item
  \textbf{Top overlap} (if \(i > 0\)):
  \(\Omega_T = O[i : i+o,; j : j+B]\)
\end{itemize}

Corresponding regions in candidate patch \(P\):
\(P_L = P[:, :o], \quad P_T = P[:o, :]\).

\textbf{Overlap Matching Error: Sum of Squared Differences (SSD)}

The \textbf{sum of squared differences (SSD)} is used as the local
matching energy. The overlapping region must minimize discrepancy, which
is implemented with the \textbf{SSD matching cost}, which is defined as:
\(E(P, O) = \sum\limits_{(x, y) \in \Omega} \| P(x, y) - O(x, y) \|^2\).
This is computed as:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Left overlap error}:
  \(E_L(P, O) = \sum\limits_{x=0}^{B-1} \sum\limits_{y=0}^{o-1} \left\| O_{i+x, j+y} - P_{x,y} \right\|_2^2\)
\item
  \textbf{Top overlap error}:
  \(E_T(P, O) = \sum\limits_{x=0}^{o-1} \sum\limits_{y=0}^{B-1} \left\| O_{i+x, j+y} - P_{x,y} \right\|_2^2\)
\item
  \textbf{Total overlap energy}:
  \(\boxed{E(P, O) = E_L(P, O) + E_T(P, O)}\)
\end{itemize}

This is implemented using the function \texttt{ssd\_patch()} in the next
code snippet.

\textbf{Patch Selection as Optimization}

At each location \((i,j)\), the algorithm solves:
\(\boxed{P^* = \arg \underset{P}{\min}E(P, O)}\), the optimal patch
minimizes this energy. This is a \textbf{local energy minimization
problem}.

\textbf{Probabilistic Patch Selection}

To avoid verbatim repetition and improve visual diversity, the algorithm
uses \textbf{ε-approximate minimization}:
\(\mathcal{P}_\varepsilon=\left\{P \mid E(P,O) \le (1+\varepsilon)\underset{P}{\min}E(P,O)\right\}\)
and samples: \(P^* \sim \text{Uniform}(\mathcal{P}_\varepsilon)\).
Rather than always choosing the global minimum, patches within a
tolerance are randomly sampled:
\(\mathcal{C} = { \mathbf{P}_k : | \text{SSD}_k - \text{SSD}_{\min} | < k \cdot \text{SSD}_{\min} }\).
This introduces \textbf{controlled randomness}, preventing verbatim
copying (refer to the next couple of lines of python code).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\ControlFlowTok{if} \BuiltInTok{abs}\NormalTok{(ds[ix] }\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{ mind) }\OperatorTok{\textless{}}\NormalTok{ k}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{mind:}
\NormalTok{    patches.append(samples[ix])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Once the patch \(P^*\) is selected, the algorithm determines a
\textbf{minimum error boundary cut} through the overlap region to ensure
seamless stitching.

\textbf{Patch Placement Rule}

Once selected: \(O[i : i+B,; j : j+B] \leftarrow P^*\) Overlaps
overwrite previous pixels, enforcing \textbf{local consistency}.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{seam-finding-via-minimum-cut-minimum-error-boundary-cut}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Seam Finding via Minimum-Cut (Minimum
Error Boundary
Cut)}}{Seam Finding via Minimum-Cut (Minimum Error Boundary Cut)}}\label{seam-finding-via-minimum-cut-minimum-error-boundary-cut}}

Even with overlap matching, hard boundaries cause visible seams,
resulting in \textbf{Boundary Artifacts}. This is mitigated with optimal
boundary cut. To avoid visible seams, the algorithm finds an optimal
path through the overlapping region that minimizes the pixel difference
between two overlapping patches \(P_1\) and \(P_2\). Define a per-pixel
error map: \(e(x,y) = \|\mathbf{P}_1(x,y) - \mathbf{P}_2(x,y)\|^2\). The
goal is to find a path \(\mathcal{S}\) through the overlap that
minimizes the cumulative error:
\(E(\mathcal{S}) = \sum\limits_{(x,y)\in\mathcal{S}} e(x,y)\), subject
to one pixel per row (vertical seam) and 4-connected or 8-connected
adjacency. This is a \textbf{shortest-path problem} solvable by dynamic
programming.

Let \(E(i,j)\) be the cumulative minimum cost up to pixel \((i,j)\), the
\textbf{dynamic programming} recurrence for the error is:
\(E(i, j) = e(i, j) + \min \{ E(i-1, j-1), E(i-1, j), E(i-1, j+1) \}\).
This ensures the cut passes through regions of minimal mismatch,
producing natural-looking transitions. The function \texttt{min\_cut()}
implements the above DP and the recurrence relation with the following
line of python code:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{E[i,j] }\OperatorTok{=} \BuiltInTok{min}\NormalTok{(E[i}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,j}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{], E[i}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,j], E[i}\OperatorTok{{-}}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{,j}\OperatorTok{+}\DecValTok{1}\NormalTok{]) }\OperatorTok{+}\NormalTok{ e[i,j]}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Backtracking recovers the optimal seam. Only pixels \textbf{to one side
of the seam} are copied from the new patch, ensuring first-order
continuity across patch boundaries (resulting in smooth blending).

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{complete-texture-synthesis-algorithm-with-image-quilting}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Complete Texture Synthesis Algorithm
with Image
Quilting}}{Complete Texture Synthesis Algorithm with Image Quilting}}\label{complete-texture-synthesis-algorithm-with-image-quilting}}

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  Extract patches from exemplar
\item
  Initialize output grid
\item
  For each patch location:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Compute SSD overlap cost
  \item
    Select candidate patches
  \item
    Compute min-cut seam
  \item
    Blend patch
  \end{itemize}
\item
  Repeat until output is filled
\end{enumerate}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

Below is the full implementation of \textbf{texture synthesis with image
quilting}, adapted from Efros \& Freeman (2001). It also visually
compares a large-size texture synthesized with random baseline
vs.~quliting algorithm.

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]

            \PY{n}{minj} \PY{o}{+}\PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
        \PY{n}{indices} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{minj}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{indices}
        \PY{n}{i} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{indices}

    \PY{n}{quilt\PYZus{}gen} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{ones}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{M}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{N}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{d}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{nan}
    \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{i} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{list}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{M} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{B} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{+} \PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{M} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{j} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{list}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{N} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{B} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{+} \PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{N} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{:}
            \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{quilt\PYZus{}gen}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape} \PY{o}{==} \PY{n}{samples}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{shape}\PY{p}{:}

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_69_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{interpretation-as-markov-random-field-mrf}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Interpretation as Markov Random Field
(MRF)}}{Interpretation as Markov Random Field (MRF)}}\label{interpretation-as-markov-random-field-mrf}}

This process approximates \textbf{MAP} inference in a \textbf{Markov
Random Field}: \(P(O) \propto \prod_{(i,j)} \exp(-E_{i,j}(P,O))\). Each
patch is conditionally dependent only on its \textbf{left and top
neighbors}: \(P(P_{i,j} \mid O_{<i,<j})\). This greedy optimization is
valid because: * dependencies are local * texture stationarity holds

\hypertarget{why-the-algorithm-works}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Why the Algorithm
Works}}{Why the Algorithm Works}}\label{why-the-algorithm-works}}

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Statistical explanation}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Texture statistics are captured by local neighborhoods
  \item
    SSD enforces local compatibility
  \item
    Random sampling prevents tiling artifacts
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Optimization view}

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    Greedy local minimization approximates global texture likelihood
  \item
    Overlap acts as a soft continuity constraint
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{texture-transfer}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{6.2.2 Texture
Transfer}}{6.2.2 Texture Transfer}}\label{texture-transfer}}

Given:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Texture image \(I\)
\item
  Target image \(T\)
\end{itemize}

Goal of texture transfer is:

\begin{quote}
Transfer the \emph{texture} of ( I ) while preserving the
\emph{structure} of ( T ).
\end{quote}

In other words, texture transfer synthesizes an image \(\mathbf{O}\)
that:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Matches the \textbf{local texture statistics} of \(\mathbf{I}\)
\item
  Resembles a \textbf{target image} \(\mathbf{T}\) globally
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{combined-energy-objective-function}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Combined Energy Objective
Function}}{Combined Energy Objective Function}}\label{combined-energy-objective-function}}

For a candidate patch \(\mathbf{P}\): \$E(\mathbf{P}) =
\lambda E\_\{\text{overlap}\}(\mathbf{P}) + (1-\lambda)
E\_\{\text{target}\}(\mathbf{P}) \$, where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The first term corresponds to \textbf{Overlap consistency}
  (\textbf{texture coherence}):
  \(E_{\text{overlap}}(P) = \sum\limits_{(x,y)\in\Omega} \| O(x,y) - P(x,y) \|_2^2\)
\item
  The second term corresponds to \textbf{Target similarity} (\textbf{in
  structure}):
  \(E_{\text{target}}(P) = \sum\limits_{x,y} \| P(x,y) - T(x+i,y+j) \|_2^2\)
\item
  \(\lambda \in [0,1]\) balances both
\end{itemize}

This is implemented using the function \texttt{texture\_transfer()} in
the next code snippet.

\hypertarget{optimization}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Optimization}}{Optimization}}\label{optimization}}

\(P^* = \arg\underset{P \in \mathcal{P}}{\min} E(P)\)

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\lambda \rightarrow 1\): pure texture synthesis
\item
  \(\lambda \rightarrow 0\): image approximation
\item
  Intermediate values: texture-painted structure
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{interpretation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Interpretation}}{Interpretation}}\label{interpretation}}

This is \textbf{patch-based conditional texture synthesis}, equivalent
to: \(P(P_{i,j} | O_{<i,<j}, T)\)

The next code snippet demonstrates how PI texture is transferred in the
face image:

                        \PY{n}{patches}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{append}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{samples}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{ix}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
                \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} choose one randomly}
                \PY{n}{index} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{random}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{choice}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{len}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{patches}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}
                \PY{n}{I2} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{patches}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{index}\PY{p}{]}
                \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{j} \PY{o}{\PYZgt{}} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{:}
                    \PY{n}{jmins} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{min\PYZus{}cut}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{out}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{I2}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{B}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
                    \PY{n}{ix} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{o}
                    \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{jmin} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n}{jmins}\PY{p}{:}
                        \PY{n}{out}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{ix}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{jmin}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{I2}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{ix}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{jmin}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} 
                        \PY{n}{ix} \PY{o}{+}\PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
                \PY{k}{else}\PY{p}{:}
                    \PY{n}{out}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{I2}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} 
                \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{i} \PY{o}{\PYZgt{}} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{:}		
                    \PY{n}{imins} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{min\PYZus{}cut}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{transpose}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{out}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{transpose}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{I2}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
                    \PY{n}{jx} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}
                    \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{imin} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n}{imins}\PY{p}{:}
                        \PY{n}{out}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{imin}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{jx}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{I2}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{imin}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{jx}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} 
                        \PY{n}{jx} \PY{o}{+}\PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}	
                \PY{k}{else}\PY{p}{:}
                    \PY{n}{out}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{I2}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{o}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} 
             \PY{k}{else}\PY{p}{:}
                \PY{n}{out}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,}\PY{p}{:}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{0} 
        
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{I}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{It}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{out}\PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{M}\PY{p}{,} \PY{p}{:}\PY{n}{N}\PY{p}{]} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}out[:i\PYZhy{}(B\PYZhy{}o),:j\PYZhy{}(B\PYZhy{}o)]}

\PY{n}{imfile} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{images/epii.png}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}
\PY{n}{imfile2} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{images/me.png}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}
\PY{n}{B} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{20}
\PY{n}{im\PYZus{}text}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{im\PYZus{}target}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{im\PYZus{}out} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{texture\PYZus{}transfer}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{imfile}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{imfile2}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{B}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{k}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{0.1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{lambda1}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mf}{0.5}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{30}{\boxspacing}

\PY{n}{plt}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{suptitle}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+sa}{f}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{Texture transfer with image quitling}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{size}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{20}\PY{p}{)}

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_72_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    Image quilting formulates texture synthesis as a \textbf{patch selection
and seam optimization problem}, grounded in local statistical
stationarity. By combining:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Overlapping patches
\item
  SSD-based similarity
\item
  Minimum-error boundary cuts
\end{itemize}

it achieves high-quality texture synthesis and transfer with remarkable
simplicity and interpretability.

    \hypertarget{graph-based-optimization-methods}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{7. Graph-Based Optimization
Methods}}{7. Graph-Based Optimization Methods}}\label{graph-based-optimization-methods}}

\textbf{Graph-based optimization methods} provide a powerful and
intuitive framework for solving many vision problems by modeling images
as graphs, where pixels or regions are represented as nodes and their
relationships as weighted edges. By formulating tasks such as
segmentation and grouping as graph partitioning or energy minimization
problems, these methods enable globally optimal or near-optimal
solutions with strong theoretical guarantees and practical efficiency.

\hypertarget{interactive-image-segmentation-with-graph-cut}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{7.1 Interactive Image
Segmentation with Graph
Cut}}{7.1 Interactive Image Segmentation with Graph Cut}}\label{interactive-image-segmentation-with-graph-cut}}

Image segmentation aims to partition an image into semantically
meaningful regions, such as foreground and background. Fully automatic
segmentation is notoriously ill-posed due to ambiguity, noise, and
complex appearance variations. \textbf{Interactive image segmentation}
alleviates this by incorporating \textbf{user-provided scribbles} that
indicate example foreground and background pixels.

Interactive image segmentation is a powerful computer vision technique
that allows users to guide the segmentation process through simple
annotations or \emph{scribbles}. The user roughly marks areas that
belong to the \textbf{foreground} (the object of interest) and
\textbf{background} (the rest of the image). These annotations provide
statistical cues that help the algorithm separate the object from the
background accurately.

One of the most elegant and effective frameworks for interactive
segmentation is \textbf{Graph-Cut--based segmentation}, popularized by
Boykov and Jolly (2001). Graph cuts formulate segmentation as a
\textbf{global energy minimization problem} that integrates:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Statistical appearance models} (likelihoods)
\item
  \textbf{Spatial smoothness constraints}
\item
  \textbf{User-provided hard constraints}
\end{itemize}

and also formulates image segmentation as a \textbf{min-cut/max-flow
optimization} problem on a graph. Each pixel in the image becomes a
node, and the edges encode both \textbf{data fidelity} (how likely a
pixel belongs to the foreground or background based on color models) and
\textbf{spatial coherence} (how likely neighboring pixels belong to the
same region).

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{bayesian-formulation-of-interactive-image-segmentation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Bayesian Formulation of Interactive
Image
Segmentation}}{Bayesian Formulation of Interactive Image Segmentation}}\label{bayesian-formulation-of-interactive-image-segmentation}}

Let an image be defined on a discrete pixel lattice
\(\mathbf{I} : \Omega \subset \mathbb{Z}^2 \rightarrow \mathbb{R}^3\),
where each pixel location is denoted by \(p = (i,j) \in \Omega\). The
goal of image segmentation is to assign each pixel a semantic label
\(L_p \in \{F, B\}\), where \(F\) denotes \emph{foreground} and \(B\)
denotes \emph{background}. Collectively, the labeling is written as
\(\mathbf{L} = {L_p \mid p \in \Omega}\). Interactive segmentation
incorporates \textbf{user-provided scribbles} as partial observations
that constrain the labeling and guide the segmentation process.

\hypertarget{maximum-a-posteriori-map-estimation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Maximum a Posteriori (MAP)
Estimation}}{Maximum a Posteriori (MAP) Estimation}}\label{maximum-a-posteriori-map-estimation}}

Let

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(\mathbf{I} = \{\mathbf{x}_p\}_{p\in\Omega}\) denote the observed
  image,
\item
  \(\mathbf{L} = \{L_p\}_{p\in\Omega}\) denote the unknown label field,
\item
  \(\mathcal{N}\) denote a neighborhood system.
\end{itemize}

Image segmentation seeks an optimal labeling
\(\hat{\mathbf{L}} = { \hat{L}_p \mid p \in \Omega }, \quad \hat{L}_p \in \mathcal{C}\),
where \(\mathcal{C}\) denotes the set of possible labels (e.g.,
foreground and background). In the Bayesian framework, this labeling is
obtained via \textbf{maximum a posteriori (MAP)} estimation:
\(\hat{\mathbf{L}} = \arg\underset{\mathbf{L}}{\max} P(\mathbf{L} \mid \mathbf{I})\).
Applying Bayes' rule yields:
\(P(\mathbf{L} \mid \mathbf{I}) = \frac{P(\mathbf{I} \mid \mathbf{L}) P(\mathbf{L})}{P(\mathbf{I})}\).
Since \(P(\mathbf{I})\) does not depend on \(\mathbf{L}\), we have
\(P(\mathbf{L} \mid \mathbf{I}) \propto P(\mathbf{I} \mid \mathbf{L}) P(\mathbf{L})\),
i.e., maximizing the posterior is equivalent to maximizing the product:
\(P(\mathbf{I} \mid \mathbf{L}) P(\mathbf{L})\), where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \(P(\mathbf{I} \mid \mathbf{L})\) is the \textbf{likelihood}, modeling
  how pixel observations agree with labels,
\item
  \(P(\mathbf{L})\) is the \textbf{prior}, encoding spatial coherence
  assumptions.
\end{itemize}

Taking the negative logarithm converts MAP estimation into an
\textbf{energy minimization problem}:
\(\hat{\mathbf{L}} = \arg\underset{\mathbf{L}}{\min} E(\mathbf{L})\),
where
\(E(\mathbf{L}) = -\log P(\mathbf{I} \mid \mathbf{L}) - \log P(\mathbf{L})\).
This transformation is fundamental: it converts probabilistic inference
into a \textbf{deterministic variational optimization problem}, enabling
the use of powerful graph-based algorithms.

\hypertarget{likelihood-term-conditional-independence}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Likelihood Term (Conditional
Independence)}}{Likelihood Term (Conditional Independence)}}\label{likelihood-term-conditional-independence}}

Assuming \textbf{conditional independence of pixels given labels}:
\(P(\mathbf{I}\mid\mathbf{L}) = \prod\limits_{p\in\Omega} P(\mathbf{x}_p \mid L_p)\).
Thus,
\(-\log P(\mathbf{I}\mid\mathbf{L}) = \sum\limits_{p\in\Omega} -\log P(\mathbf{x}_p \mid L_p) = \sum\limits_{p\in\Omega} D_p(L_p)\),
where \(\boxed{D_p(L_p) = -\log P(\mathbf{x}_p \mid L_p)}\) is the
\textbf{data (unary) term}, measures how well pixel \(p\) agrees with
label \(L_p\), where \(\mathbf{x}_p \in \mathbb{R}^3\) is the observed
feature vector at pixel \(p\).

\hypertarget{prior-term-markov-random-field-assumption}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Prior Term (Markov Random Field
Assumption)}}{Prior Term (Markov Random Field Assumption)}}\label{prior-term-markov-random-field-assumption}}

To make the problem tractable, the labeling \(\mathbf{L}\) is modeled as
a \textbf{Markov Random Field (MRF)} defined on the image grid. Under
the Markov assumption, the prior distribution factorizes over local
neighborhoods:
\(P(\mathbf{L}) \propto \prod\limits_{(p,q)\in\mathcal{N}} \psi_{pq}(L_p, L_q)\),
where \(\mathcal{N}\) denotes a predefined neighborhood system (e.g., 4-
or 8-connected), and \(\psi_{pq}\) are pairwise potential
(compatibility) functions, so that
\(-\log P(\mathbf{L}) = -\sum\limits_{(p,q)\in\mathcal{N}} \log \psi_{pq}(L_p, L_q)\).

Also, since the label field \(\mathbf{L}\) is a \textbf{Markov Random
Field} with respect to \(\mathcal{N}\), by the
\textbf{Hammersley--Clifford theorem}, its Gibbs distribution is:
\(P(\mathbf{L})=\frac{1}{Z}\exp\Big(-\sum\limits_{(p,q)\in\mathcal{N}} V_{pq}(L_p,L_q)\Big)\).
Hence,
\(-\log P(\mathbf{L}) = \sum\limits_{(p,q)\in\mathcal{N}} V_{pq}(L_p,L_q) + \log Z\).
Since \(\log Z\) is constant w.r.t. \(\mathbf{L}\), it can be dropped.

This is the \textbf{smoothness term} encodes the prior belief that
neighboring pixels are likely to share the same label:
\(\boxed{V_{pq}(L_p, L_q) = -\log \psi_{pq}(L_p, L_q)}\). A widely used
choice for the spatial coherence is the \textbf{contrast-sensitive Potts
model}:
\(V_{pq}(L_p,L_q) = \begin{cases} w_{pq}, & L_p \neq L_q, \\ 0, & L_p = L_q, \end{cases}\)
where the edge weight is defined as:
\(w_{pq} = \exp\left( -\frac{|\mathbf{x}_p - \mathbf{x}_q|^2}{2\sigma^2} \right)\).
where the weight \(w_{pq}\) depends on the similarity between
neighboring pixels. This formulation encourages \textbf{piecewise-smooth
segmentations} while allowing discontinuities at strong image edges.
This penalizes label discontinuities more strongly between pixels with
similar luminance values.

\hypertarget{final-energy-expression}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Final Energy
Expression}}{Final Energy Expression}}\label{final-energy-expression}}

Combining likelihood (unary data) and prior (pairwise smoothness) terms
yields the final variational problem:
\(\boxed{ \hat{\mathbf{L}} = \arg\underset{\mathbf{L}}{\min}E[L]}\)
where
\(\boxed{E(\mathbf{L}) = \sum\limits_{p\in\Omega} D_p(L_p) + \sum\limits_{(p,q)\in\mathcal{N}} V_{pq}(L_p,L_q)}\).
This is the standard \textbf{MRF energy functional} minimized by graph
cuts. This energy is submodular for binary labels under the Potts model,
which guarantees that the global minimum can be found efficiently using
graph-cut algorithms.

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The data term pulls each pixel toward the label most consistent with
  its appearance.
\item
  The smoothness term couples neighboring labels to enforce spatial
  coherence.
\item
  The total energy represents a trade-off between data fidelity and
  regularization.
\item
  MAP inference is therefore exactly equivalent to minimizing a
  well-defined energy functional.
\item
  This equivalence forms the theoretical foundation of graph-cut-based
  interactive segmentation methods such as Boykov--Jolly and GrabCut,
  enabling globally optimal solutions under binary labeling constraints.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{feature-representation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Feature
Representation}}{Feature Representation}}\label{feature-representation}}

In the implementation, the RGB image is converted to the \textbf{YIQ
color space}:
\(\mathbf{x}_p = \begin{bmatrix} Y(p) \\ I(p) \\ Q(p) \end{bmatrix}\),
which approximately decorrelates luminance and chrominance, improving
Gaussian modeling of color distributions.

\hypertarget{statistical-modeling-from-scribbles}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Statistical Modeling from
Scribbles}}{Statistical Modeling from Scribbles}}\label{statistical-modeling-from-scribbles}}

Let \(\mathcal{S}_c \subset \Omega\) denote the set of pixels marked by
user scribbles as belonging to class \(c \in {F,B}\).

\hypertarget{gaussian-appearance-model}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Gaussian Appearance
Model}}{Gaussian Appearance Model}}\label{gaussian-appearance-model}}

Each class is modeled as a multivariate Gaussian:
\(P(\mathbf{x}_p \mid c) = \mathcal{N}(\mathbf{x}_p;,\boldsymbol{\mu}_c,\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_c)\).

\hypertarget{mean-and-covariance-estimation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Mean and Covariance
Estimation}}{Mean and Covariance Estimation}}\label{mean-and-covariance-estimation}}

Given scribble samples \({\mathbf{x}_p : p \in \mathcal{S}_c}\), the
parameters are estimated (with \textbf{MLE} of \textbf{Gaussian}
parameters) as:

\(\boldsymbol{\mu}_c = \frac{1}{|\mathcal{S}_c|}\)
\(\sum\limits_{p \in \mathcal{S}_c} \mathbf{x}_p\),

\(\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_c = \frac{1}{|\mathcal{S}_c|-1} \sum\limits_{p \in \mathcal{S}_c} (\mathbf{x}_p-\boldsymbol{\mu}_c)(\mathbf{x}_p-\boldsymbol{\mu}_c)^\top\).

This matches the implementation:

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{µ[c] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.mean(comps[c], axis}\OperatorTok{=}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{)}
\NormalTok{Σ[c] }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ np.cov(comps[c].T)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

When all scribble colors are identical, the covariance becomes singular.
In this case, the likelihood collapses to a \textbf{deterministic
assignment}, which is explicitly handled in the code.

\hypertarget{gaussian-likelihood}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Gaussian
Likelihood}}{Gaussian Likelihood}}\label{gaussian-likelihood}}

For non-degenerate covariance, the likelihood is:
\(P(\mathbf{x}_p \mid c) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{(2\pi)^3 |\boldsymbol{\Sigma}_c|}} \exp\left( -\frac{1}{2} (\mathbf{x}_p-\boldsymbol{\mu}_c)^\top \boldsymbol{\Sigma}_c^{-1} (\mathbf{x}_p-\boldsymbol{\mu}_c) \right)\).

Posterior class probabilities are normalized per pixel:
\(\pi_c(p) = P(c \mid \mathbf{x}_p) = \frac{P(\mathbf{x}_p \mid c)} {\sum\limits_{c'} P(\mathbf{x}_p \mid c')}\)
(with Bayes' Rule).

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{graph-construction}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Graph
Construction}}{Graph Construction}}\label{graph-construction}}

The energy minimization problem is solved by constructing a
\textbf{directed flow graph}:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  One node per pixel \(p\)
\item
  Two terminal nodes:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    \textbf{Source (S)} → foreground
  \item
    \textbf{Sink (T)} → background
  \end{itemize}
\end{itemize}

\hypertarget{terminal-edges-unary-costs}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Terminal Edges (Unary
Costs)}}{Terminal Edges (Unary Costs)}}\label{terminal-edges-unary-costs}}

Edges connecting pixels to terminals encode data costs:
\(\text{cap}(S \rightarrow p) = D_p(F), \quad \text{cap}(p \rightarrow T) = D_p(B)\).

User scribbles impose \textbf{hard constraints} by forcing terminal
connections with dominant capacities.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{g.add\_tedge(nid, πs[k0][i,j], πs[k1][i,j])}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{neighborhood-edges-pairwise-costs}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Neighborhood Edges (Pairwise
Costs)}}{Neighborhood Edges (Pairwise Costs)}}\label{neighborhood-edges-pairwise-costs}}

Edges between neighboring pixels encode smoothness:
\(\text{cap}(p \leftrightarrow q) = w_{pq}\). The pairwise smoothness
cost is computed exclusively from the luminance component \(Y\), i.e.,
\(w_{pq} = \exp\left(-\frac{(Y_p - Y_q)^2}{2\sigma^2}\right)\), so
chrominance differences do not influence boundary penalties.

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{wt }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ exp(}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{(yuv[i,j,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{]}\OperatorTok{{-}}\NormalTok{yuv[i1,j1,}\DecValTok{0}\NormalTok{])}\OperatorTok{**}\DecValTok{2} \OperatorTok{/}\NormalTok{ (}\DecValTok{2}\OperatorTok{*}\NormalTok{σ}\OperatorTok{**}\DecValTok{2}\NormalTok{))}
\NormalTok{g.add\_edge(n1, n2, wt, wt)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

\hypertarget{optimization-via-max-flow-min-cut}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Optimization via Max-Flow /
Min-Cut}}{Optimization via Max-Flow / Min-Cut}}\label{optimization-via-max-flow-min-cut}}

By the \textbf{Max-Flow Min-Cut Theorem}, the minimum cut of this graph
yields the labeling:
\(\mathbf{L}^* = \arg\underset{\mathbf{L}}{\min} E(\mathbf{L})\).

\begin{Shaded}
\begin{Highlighting}[]
\NormalTok{g.maxflow()}
\NormalTok{sgm }\OperatorTok{=}\NormalTok{ g.get\_grid\_segments(nodeids)}
\end{Highlighting}
\end{Shaded}

Pixels connected to the source belong to the foreground; those connected
to the sink belong to the background.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{algorithm-overview-interactive-graph-cut-segmentation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Algorithm Overview: Interactive
Graph-Cut
Segmentation}}{Algorithm Overview: Interactive Graph-Cut Segmentation}}\label{algorithm-overview-interactive-graph-cut-segmentation}}

The \textbf{Graph Cut} algorithm efficiently computes the globally
optimal binary labeling (for two labels: foreground/background) by
constructing a graph and finding its \textbf{minimum cut}---the smallest
set of edges that separates the graph into two disjoint sets
corresponding to the foreground and background. The steps to achieve
interactive segmentation using Graph Cut are:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Collect User Scribbles:} The user marks foreground and
  background pixels on the image (these act as constraints).
\item
  \textbf{Color Model Estimation:} Gaussian models (\(\beta\),
  \(\Sigma\)) for foreground and background are estimated from the
  user-marked regions.
\item
  \textbf{Likelihood Computation:} For each pixel, compute the
  probability of belonging to the foreground or background using the
  Gaussian densities (estimate class Gaussian models).
\item
  \textbf{Graph Construction:}

  \begin{itemize}
  \item
    Each pixel is a node.
  \item
    Two terminal nodes represent the \textbf{source (foreground)} and
    \textbf{sink (background)}.
  \item
    Edge weights encode:

    \begin{itemize}
    \tightlist
    \item
      \textbf{Data terms} (connection to source/sink based on
      likelihoods).
    \item
      \textbf{Smoothness terms} (connections between neighboring
      pixels).
    \end{itemize}
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Max-Flow/Min-Cut Computation:} Using the
  \textbf{Boykov-Kolmogorov} or \textbf{Push-Relabel} algorithm, the
  minimum cut of the graph is computed, separating the image into
  foreground and background.
\item
  \textbf{Segmentation Output Extraction:} The resulting mask indicates
  the segmented foreground object.
\end{enumerate}

The next code implements graphcut segmentation using the python library
\texttt{maxflow} and visualizes the class-conditional densities learnt
from the scribbles and the segmented output.

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{27}{\boxspacing}

        \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{j} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n}{indices}\PY{p}{:}
            \PY{k}{if} \PY{n+nb}{abs}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y1}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o}{\PYZlt{}}\PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1} \PY{o+ow}{and} \PY{n}{y1}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{\PYZgt{}} \PY{l+m+mi}{0} \PY{o+ow}{and} \PY{n}{y1}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{\PYZlt{}} \PY{n}{h}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1} \PY{o+ow}{and} \PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{\PYZgt{}} \PY{l+m+mi}{0} \PY{o+ow}{and} \PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{\PYZlt{}} \PY{n}{h}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{:}
                \PY{n}{bgnodes}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{add}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{ij2id}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{x1}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{y1}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{w}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
                \PY{n}{k} \PY{o}{+}\PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
                \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{k} \PY{o}{==} \PY{n}{num}\PY{p}{:}

                \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{j1} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{j}\PY{o}{+}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
                    \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{i1} \PY{o}{==} \PY{n}{i} \PY{o+ow}{and} \PY{n}{j1} \PY{o}{==} \PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{:}
                        \PY{k}{continue}
                    \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{i1} \PY{o}{\PYZgt{}}\PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{0} \PY{o+ow}{and} \PY{n}{i1} \PY{o}{\PYZlt{}} \PY{n}{w} \PY{o+ow}{and} \PY{n}{j1} \PY{o}{\PYZgt{}}\PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{0} \PY{o+ow}{and} \PY{n}{j1} \PY{o}{\PYZlt{}} \PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{:}
                        \PY{n}{wt} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{exp}\PY{p}{(}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{yuv}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{n}{yuv}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i1}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j1}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2} \PY{o}{/} \PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{σ}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)} 
                        \PY{n}{n1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{n2} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{ij2id}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{w}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{ij2id}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{i1}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{j1}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{w}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{)}
                        \PY{n}{g}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{add\PYZus{}edge}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{n2}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{wt}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{wt}\PY{p}{)} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}capacity		}
                        \PY{n}{edges}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{add}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{str}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n+nb}{str}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
                        \PY{n}{edges}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{add}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{str}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n+nb}{str}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}


    \PY{n}{plot\PYZus{}dist}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{rgb}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{comps}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{µ}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{Σ}\PY{p}{)}

    \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Find the maxiµm flow.}
    \PY{n}{g}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{maxflow}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} Get the segments of the nodes in the grid.}
    \PY{n}{sgm} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{g}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{get\PYZus{}grid\PYZus{}segments}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{nodeids}\PY{p}{)}	
    \PY{n}{img\PYZus{}seg} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{int\PYZus{}}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{sgm}\PY{p}{)} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}np.logical\PYZus{}not(sgm))}

    \PY{n}{idx} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{img\PYZus{}seg}\PY{o}{==}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{rgb\PYZus{}seg} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{deepcopy}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{rgb}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{n}{rgb\PYZus{}seg}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{idx}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mf}{1.} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}255 \PYZsh{}1.}
    
    \PY{n}{plot\PYZus{}fg\PYZus{}bg}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{πs}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{img\PYZus{}seg}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{rgb\PYZus{}seg}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{comps}\PY{p}{)}

    \hypertarget{code-explanation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Code
Explanation}}{Code Explanation}}\label{code-explanation}}

Below is the Python implementation of \textbf{Interactive Image
Segmentation using Graph Cut}, which demonstrates these steps in action.
The implementation uses:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{numpy} and \texttt{scipy} for numerical operations,
\item
  \texttt{matplotlib} for visualization,
\item
  \texttt{maxflow} (PyMaxflow library) for the graph optimization,
\item
  \texttt{graphviz} for illustrating the flow graph, and
\item
  \texttt{skimage} for simple image transformations.
\end{itemize}

Let's break down the key components of the implementation.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{image-loading-and-preprocessing}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Image Loading and
Preprocessing}}{Image Loading and Preprocessing}}\label{image-loading-and-preprocessing}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The function \texttt{mpimg.imread()} loads the input image and its
  \emph{scribed} version (user markings).
\item
  The RGB image is converted to \textbf{YIQ color space} for better
  luminance-chrominance separation, using the function
  \texttt{rgb2yiq()}.
\item
  The function \texttt{find\_marked\_locations()} identifies user-marked
  pixels that differ between the original and the scribed image.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{foreground-and-background-modeling}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Foreground and Background
Modeling}}{Foreground and Background Modeling}}\label{foreground-and-background-modeling}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The function \texttt{compute\_pdfs()} collects color samples from
  user-marked regions.
\item
  It estimates \textbf{mean (µ)} and \textbf{covariance (Σ)} for each
  component (foreground and background).
\item
  These form simple \textbf{Gaussian color models}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{building-the-graph}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Building the
Graph}}{Building the Graph}}\label{building-the-graph}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  A grid graph is created where each pixel is represented as a node.
\item
  Source (foreground) and sink (background) nodes are added implicitly
  via \textbf{t-edges}.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{adding-edge-weights}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Adding Edge
Weights}}{Adding Edge Weights}}\label{adding-edge-weights}}

Each pixel has two types of edges:

\begin{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Terminal Edges (Data Term):}

  These connect pixels to source and sink, with weights based on the
  foreground and background probabilities.
\item
  \textbf{Neighborhood Edges (Smoothness Term):}

  These connect neighboring pixels, enforcing smooth boundaries.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{running-max-flow-min-cut}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Running Max-Flow /
Min-Cut}}{Running Max-Flow / Min-Cut}}\label{running-max-flow-min-cut}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The \textbf{max-flow} algorithm computes the minimum cut efficiently.
\item
  \texttt{sgm} is the segmentation mask indicating which pixels belong
  to the source (foreground).
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{visualization}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Visualization}}{Visualization}}\label{visualization}}

The code visualizes:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Foreground/Background distributions,
\item
  Pixel-wise probability maps,
\item
  Final segmentation mask, and
\item
  The flow graph structure.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{results}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Results}}{Results}}\label{results}}

The user can try different pairs of \texttt{imfile} and
\texttt{imfile\_scribe}. Upon running \texttt{segment\_graph\_cut()} the
output will show:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  The \textbf{input image},
\item
  The \textbf{foreground and background histograms},
\item
  The \textbf{segmentation mask}, and
\item
  The \textbf{segmented image} highlighting the extracted object.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{discussion}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Discussion}}{Discussion}}\label{discussion}}

This implementation demonstrates how user interaction can guide an
otherwise ill-posed segmentation problem toward a robust solution. Graph
cuts provide:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Global optimization} under binary labeling,
\item
  \textbf{Flexible modeling} with Gaussian priors, and
\item
  \textbf{Efficient computation} via max-flow algorithms.
\end{itemize}

This forms the foundation of more advanced systems such as
\textbf{GrabCut}, which iteratively refines the segmentation using
Expectation-Maximization and Graph Cuts.

\hypertarget{key-properties}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Key
Properties}}{Key Properties}}\label{key-properties}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Globally optimal for binary segmentation
\item
  Statistically grounded Bayesian formulation
\item
  Robust to sparse user input
\item
  Efficient via max-flow algorithms
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{replacing-the-background-of-an-image}{%
\subsubsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Replacing the Background of an
Image}}{Replacing the Background of an Image}}\label{replacing-the-background-of-an-image}}

The next code snippet implements a function \texttt{change\_bg()} which
takes the \textbf{binary segmentation mask} \texttt{seg\_out} (e.g.,
from graph cuts), and wherever the mask equals \texttt{1} (the
foreground), it \textbf{keeps the original image}, while everywhere else
it uses the \textbf{new background image}. At the foreground pixels, it
blends the original with the new background using a weight
\texttt{λ\ =\ 0.9} for smoother boundaries.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{how-the-code-works}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{How the code
works}}{How the code works}}\label{how-the-code-works}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \texttt{idx\ =\ (seg\_out\ ==\ 1)} creates a boolean map indicating
  \textbf{foreground pixels}.
\item
  \texttt{output\ =\ deepcopy(new\_image)} starts with the new
  background everywhere.
\item
  The line
  \texttt{output{[}idx{]}\ =\ (1-lambda)*new\_image{[}idx{]}\ +\ lambda*orig\_image{[}idx{]}}
  replaces only the selected (foreground) pixels with a \textbf{blend}:
  \(\text{output}(i) = (1-\lambda).\text{new}(i) + \lambda.\text{orig}(i)\),
  meaning the subject stays from the original image, smoothly mixed with
  the new background to avoid sharp seams. Thus the mask directs
  \emph{where} to keep the original image, and the blending prevents
  jagged edges.
\end{itemize}

    \begin{tcolorbox}[breakable, size=fbox, boxrule=1pt, pad at break*=1mm,colback=cellbackground, colframe=cellborder]
\prompt{In}{incolor}{34}{\boxspacing}

\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

    \begin{center}
    \adjustimage{max size={0.9\linewidth}{0.9\paperheight}}{Chapter3_files/Chapter3_77_0.png}
    \end{center}
    { \hspace*{\fill} \\}
    
    \hypertarget{graph-based-image-segmentation-the-felzenszwalbhuttenlocher-algorithm}{%
\subsection{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{7.2 Graph-Based Image Segmentation:
The Felzenszwalb--Huttenlocher
Algorithm}}{7.2 Graph-Based Image Segmentation: The Felzenszwalb--Huttenlocher Algorithm}}\label{graph-based-image-segmentation-the-felzenszwalbhuttenlocher-algorithm}}

Graph-based image segmentation provides a powerful and conceptually
elegant framework for partitioning an image into perceptually coherent
regions. Rather than imposing a fixed scale or explicitly modeling
object shapes, these methods rely on \textbf{local evidence of
similarity} and a \textbf{global consistency criterion} to adaptively
determine region boundaries. Among these approaches, the algorithm
proposed by \textbf{Felzenszwalb and Huttenlocher (2004)} stands out for
its simplicity, computational efficiency, and strong empirical
performance.

At its core, the method formulates segmentation as a \textbf{graph
partitioning problem}, where pixels are treated as nodes and local pixel
dissimilarities define weighted edges. Regions are formed by
progressively merging pixels and components according to a principled
predicate that compares \textbf{inter-region dissimilarity} with
\textbf{intra-region variability}, yielding segmentations that naturally
adapt to image content and scale.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{graph-construction}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Graph
Construction}}{Graph Construction}}\label{graph-construction}}

Let an image be defined on a discrete pixel lattice
\(\mathbf{I} : \Omega \subset \mathbb{Z}^2 \rightarrow \mathbb{R}^3\).
We construct an undirected weighted graph \(G = (V, E)\), where:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Each pixel \(p \in \Omega\) corresponds to a node \(v_p \in V\).
\item
  Edges connect spatially neighboring pixels, typically using a
  \textbf{4-connected or 8-connected neighborhood}.
\item
  Each edge \((p,q) \in E\) is assigned a nonnegative weight \(w(p,q)\)
  measuring the \textbf{dissimilarity} between pixels \(p\) and \(q\).
\end{itemize}

In its simplest form, the dissimilarity is computed from the
\textbf{luminance channel}: \(w(p,q) = |Y_p - Y_q|\), where \(Y\)
denotes luminance. Using luminance alone often suffices to capture
perceptually salient boundaries while keeping the algorithm efficient
and robust.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{sorting-edges-by-dissimilarity}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Sorting Edges by
Dissimilarity}}{Sorting Edges by Dissimilarity}}\label{sorting-edges-by-dissimilarity}}

Once the graph is constructed, all edges are sorted in
\textbf{non-decreasing order of weight}:
\(w(e_1) \le w(e_2) \le \dots \le w(e_{|E|})\). This ordering ensures
that the algorithm considers \textbf{strong local similarities first},
allowing coherent regions to form before confronting larger
discontinuities.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{region-representation-and-internal-difference}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Region Representation and Internal
Difference}}{Region Representation and Internal Difference}}\label{region-representation-and-internal-difference}}

Initially, each pixel forms its own region (connected component). As the
algorithm proceeds, regions grow by merging neighboring components. For
each region \(C\), two quantities are maintained:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Region Size}: \(|C| = \text{number of pixels in } C\).
\item
  \textbf{Internal Difference}:
  \(\mathrm{Int}(C) = \underset{(u,v)\in \mathrm{MST(C)}}{\max} w(u,v)\),
  where \(\mathrm{MST}(C)\) is the minimum spanning tree of region
  \(C\).
\end{enumerate}

Intuitively, \(\mathrm{Int}(C)\) captures the \textbf{largest
dissimilarity tolerated within the region} and serves as a measure of
the region's internal variability. In practice, this value is updated
incrementally during merging by tracking the maximum edge weight used to
connect the component.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{adaptive-threshold-function}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Adaptive Threshold
Function}}{Adaptive Threshold Function}}\label{adaptive-threshold-function}}

To regulate merging behavior across different scales, Felzenszwalb and
Huttenlocher introduce a \textbf{size-dependent threshold}:
\(\tau(C) = \frac{k}{|C|}\), where \(k > 0\) is a global scale
parameter.

This function has a crucial effect:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Small regions} are given higher tolerance and can grow more
  easily.
\item
  \textbf{Large regions} are more conservative and merge only if
  strongly supported by low dissimilarity.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{merge-predicate}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Merge
Predicate}}{Merge Predicate}}\label{merge-predicate}}

The heart of the algorithm is the \textbf{merge criterion}. Consider an
edge \((p,q)\) connecting two distinct components \(C_p\) and \(C_q\).
These components are merged if and only if:
\(w(p,q) \le \min\big( \mathrm{Int}(C_p) + \tau(C_p), \mathrm{Int}(C_q) + \tau(C_q) \big)\).

\hypertarget{interpretation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Interpretation}}{Interpretation}}\label{interpretation}}

This condition states that two regions should be merged if the
dissimilarity between them is \textbf{not significantly greater} than
the internal variability already present within each region, adjusted by
a size-dependent tolerance. As a result:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  True boundaries with strong contrast are preserved.
\item
  Homogeneous areas naturally coalesce into larger regions.
\item
  The segmentation adapts to both fine and coarse image structures.
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{single-pass-efficient-algorithm}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Single-Pass Efficient
Algorithm}}{Single-Pass Efficient Algorithm}}\label{single-pass-efficient-algorithm}}

Edges are processed \textbf{once}, in sorted order. Each merge is
implemented using a union--find (disjoint-set) data structure, yielding
an overall time complexity of: \(\mathcal{O}(|E| \log |E|)\), dominated
by edge sorting. For grid graphs, this is effectively
\textbf{near-linear in the number of pixels}, making the method suitable
for large images.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{producing-the-final-segmentation}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Producing the Final
Segmentation}}{Producing the Final Segmentation}}\label{producing-the-final-segmentation}}

After all edges have been processed, the connected components of the
graph define the final segmentation. Each component corresponds to a
region that is:

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  Internally coherent,
\item
  Separated from neighboring regions by perceptually significant
  boundaries.
\end{itemize}

For visualization or downstream processing, each region can be assigned
a representative color, such as the \textbf{mean RGB value} of its
pixels.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{key-properties-of-the-method}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Key Properties of the
Method}}{Key Properties of the Method}}\label{key-properties-of-the-method}}

\begin{itemize}
\tightlist
\item
  \textbf{Adaptive scale:} Region size emerges naturally from image
  content.
\item
  \textbf{Strong boundary preservation:} Merges respect local contrast.
\item
  \textbf{Computational efficiency:} Single-pass processing with simple
  operations.
\item
  \textbf{Minimal parameterization:} Only one intuitive parameter ( k ).
\end{itemize}

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

\hypertarget{conceptual-significance}{%
\paragraph{\texorpdfstring{\textbf{Conceptual
Significance}}{Conceptual Significance}}\label{conceptual-significance}}

The Felzenszwalb--Huttenlocher algorithm occupies a unique position
between local filtering methods and global optimization approaches such
as normalized cuts or graph cuts. Rather than solving a global energy
minimization problem, it employs a \textbf{greedy but theoretically
grounded merging strategy} that achieves an excellent balance between
accuracy and efficiency. This makes graph-based segmentation an ideal
baseline for many computer vision pipelines and a foundational method
for understanding modern region-based image analysis.

\begin{center}\rule{0.5\linewidth}{0.5pt}\end{center}

The next code snippet is a direct realization of the FH (2004) efficient
graph-based segmentation algorithm, following the exact steps of graph
construction, edge sorting, component merging, and region output.

    \PY{k}{for} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{wt}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{n1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{n2}\PY{p}{)} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n}{W}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{C1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{C2} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{n1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{n2}\PY{p}{]} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} id1, id2}
        \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{C1} \PY{o}{!=} \PY{n}{C2}\PY{p}{:}
            \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{wt} \PY{o}{\PYZlt{}} \PY{n}{min\PYZus{}int}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{C1}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{C2}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{M}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{S}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{k}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
                \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{n1} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n}{nodes} \PY{o+ow}{and} \PY{n}{n2} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n}{nodes}\PY{p}{:}
                    \PY{n}{s}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{edge}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{str}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n1}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n+nb}{str}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{n2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{color}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{red}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{weight}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{2}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{label}\PY{o}{=}\PY{n+nb}{str}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{round}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{wt}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{10}\PY{o}{*}\PY{o}{*}\PY{l+m+mi}{3}\PY{p}{,}\PY{l+m+mi}{2}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
                \PY{n}{comps}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C1}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{+}\PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{comps}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C2}\PY{p}{]}
                \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{n} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n}{comps}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C2}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{:}
                    \PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{n}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{n1}\PY{p}{]}
                \PY{n}{M}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C1}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n+nb}{max}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{M}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{M}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C2}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{wt}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
                \PY{n}{S}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C1}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{S}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C1}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{+} \PY{n}{S}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C2}\PY{p}{]}
                \PY{k}{del} \PY{n}{comps}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C2}\PY{p}{]}		
                \PY{k}{del} \PY{n}{M}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C2}\PY{p}{]}
                \PY{k}{del} \PY{n}{S}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C2}\PY{p}{]}

    \PY{n}{M1} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{\PYZob{}}\PY{p}{\PYZcb{}}
    \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{key} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n}{comps}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{keys}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{M1}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{key}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{mean}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{array}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{rgb}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{n}\PY{o}{/}\PY{o}{/}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{n}\PY{o}{\PYZpc{}}\PY{k}{h}, :] for n in comps[key]]), axis=0)
    \PY{n}{out} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{zeros}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{w}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{,} \PY{l+m+mi}{3}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}
    \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{n} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{w}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
        \PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{y} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{n} \PY{o}{/}\PY{o}{/} \PY{n}{h}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{n} \PY{o}{\PYZpc{}} \PY{n}{h}
        \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}out[x, y, :] = M[C[n]]}
        \PY{n}{out}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{M1}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{C}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{n}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{]} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}np.mean(M1[C[n]])}

    \PY{n}{s}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{render}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{mst}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{view}\PY{o}{=}\PY{k+kc}{False}\PY{p}{)}
    
    \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{rgb}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{out}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{comps}

\PY{n}{k} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mf}{.001}  \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}.0005 \PYZsh{}.001 \PYZsh{}.01 \PYZsh{}1.}
\PY{n}{imfile} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{images/hand.png}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}} \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{} \PYZsq{}hand.png\PYZsq{} \PYZsh{} \PYZsq{}building.png\PYZsq{} \PYZsh{} \PYZsh{}\PYZsq{}people.png\PYZsq{} \PYZsh{} \PYZsh{}\PYZsq{}hill.png\PYZsq{} \PYZsh{}\PYZsq{}road.png\PYZsq{} \PYZsh{}\PYZsq{}umbc.png\PYZsq{} \PYZsh{}\PYZsq{}parrot.png\PYZsq{} \PYZsq{}player.png\PYZsq{} }
\PY{n}{im\PYZus{}in}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{im\PYZus{}out}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{comp} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{seg\PYZus{}graph}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{imfile}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{k}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}
\end{tcolorbox}

    The above code smooths the hand input image, builds a pixel adjacency
graph weighted by luminance differences, greedily merges components
using the Felzenszwalb--Huttenlocher predicate, and renders the final
segmentation by coloring each region with its mean color. Here are the
key steps implemented in the above code:

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\item
  \textbf{Image loading and smoothing} The input RGB image is read and
  \textbf{Gaussian-smoothed} channel-wise to suppress noise and prevent
  spurious small regions: \(I \leftarrow I * \mathcal{G}_{\sigma}\)
  implemented via 2D convolution with a Gaussian kernel.
\item
  \textbf{Color-space conversion} The smoothed image is converted from
  RGB to \textbf{YIQ}, isolating luminance \(Y\) for boundary detection.
\item
  \textbf{Graph construction} Each pixel is treated as a graph node, and
  edges are added between \textbf{8-connected neighbors} with weights
  \(w(p,q) = |Y_p - Y_q|\), representing local dissimilarity.
\item
  \textbf{Edge sorting} All edges are sorted in \textbf{non-decreasing
  order of weight}, ensuring that the most similar pixels are considered
  first for merging.
\item
  \textbf{Component initialization} Each pixel starts as its own
  component, with:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    size \(|C| = 1\)
  \item
    internal difference \(\mathrm{Int}(C) = 0\)
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Greedy component merging} For each edge \((p,q)\) in sorted
  order:

  \begin{itemize}
  \tightlist
  \item
    identify the components \(C_p\) and \(C_q\)
  \item
    merge them if
    \(w(p,q) < \min\big(\mathrm{Int}(C_p) + \tfrac{k}{|C_p|},; \mathrm{Int}(C_q) + \tfrac{k}{|C_q|}\big)\)\\
  \item
    update component size and internal difference after merging.
  \end{itemize}
\item
  \textbf{Component statistics update} After all merges, compute a
  representative color (mean RGB) for each component.
\item
  \textbf{Region reconstruction} Construct the output image by assigning
  every pixel the color of its final component, producing a
  \textbf{piecewise-constant segmented image}.
\item
  \textbf{Optional visualization} A small subset of edges and merges is
  visualized using Graphviz to illustrate the evolving
  minimum-spanning-tree structure.
\end{enumerate}

Now, let's plot the original input and the segmented output images
side-by-side to compare them, using the next code snippet:
